10020-8{_}970{_}The mantel is exquisitely carved marble, with an ormolu frieze.
10020-8{_}971{_}On one side you will note a small piano; it is a French one, of very clear and fine tone, and beautifully finished in every respect.
10020-8{_}972{_}In this room Her Majesty the Queen may be imagined enjoying the balls given to the youthful aristocracy, something different to the State dances in the larger room; and, doubtless, by a long way, much more enjoyable.
10020-8{_}973{_}By the time the Queen can command the State balls, she will have commenced to feel the cares of her position; and will look back with real regret to the assemblies here, when she had merely to enjoy herself, a devoted mother observing the graver duties, her own greatest trouble, perhaps, being the acquirement of the tasks assigned by the governess and masters.
10020-8{_}974{_}[Illustration: THE LARGE DINING-ROOM.
10020-8{_}975{_}_From a Photo by Gunn & Stuart, Richmond._]  The large dining-room has some fine family portraits on its walls.
10020-8{_}976{_}The first you will notice is that of William II., on horseback, leading an attack; the artist (Keirzer) has produced a first-rate work of both man and horse.
10020-8{_}977{_}Underneath this picture stands the favourite horse of William II., one which carried him through numerous engagements, and earned from his Royal master a gratitude and affection that caused him to wish for his preservation in a position where he would constantly be reminded of him.
10020-8{_}978{_}[Illustration: FAVOURITE HORSE OF WILLIAM II.
10020-8{_}979{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}980{_}by Gunn & Stuart, Richmond._]  The ceiling of this room shows some beautiful relief carving of fruit and flowers, also some fine fresco work; the chandeliers here are massive, as is the furniture and other appointments.
10020-8{_}981{_}The room is long and of not much width, but lofty and well-lighted.
10020-8{_}982{_}The buffet adjoining the dining-room has some very costly and, at the same time, some very interesting contents.
10020-8{_}983{_}The Empire furniture is draped in rich crimson silk, the walls being covered with silk brocade of the same colour.
10020-8{_}984{_}The chimney-piece of sculptured marble, with an ormolu frieze, holds some choice antique porcelain vases and a valuable Roman timepiece.
10020-8{_}985{_}A massive chandelier hangs from the centre of a ceiling wrought with the arms of the house--this chandelier being solid silver.
10020-8{_}986{_}It was presented by the inhabitants of Amsterdam, while two silver lustres at the sides of the fireplace were presented by Rotterdam.
10020-8{_}987{_}Two exquisite statues stand in front of the windows, one of Venus, the other Diana, midway between which is an immense porcelain vase on a pedestal.
10020-8{_}988{_}This you will note in the view given of the room.
10020-8{_}989{_}It has special interest just now, as it was given by Marshal MacMahon, whose death recently occurred, and whose funeral--a State military one--I had the opportunity of witnessing a few weeks ago in Paris.
10020-8{_}990{_}[Illustration: THE CRYSTAL ROOM.
10020-8{_}991{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}992{_}by Gunn & Stuart, Richmond._]  The windows are of very fine stained glass, the different panes giving portraits of Kings and Princes, under each being depicted battles they had fought.
10020-8{_}993{_}Note this rare Florentine mosaic table with pedestal of ormolu; then we will pass on to the crystal room, an ante-room to the ball-room.
10020-8{_}994{_}Some immense candelabra of purest crystal at once attracted my attention; not only were they of the largest I had ever seen, but they were absolutely unique in composition: the pedestals in support were ormolu and marble.
10020-8{_}995{_}[Illustration: SIDEBOARD AND MINIATURES IN SMALL DINING-ROOM.
10020-8{_}996{_}_From a Photo by Gunn & Stuart, Richmond._]  The appointments here are again in the First Empire style.
10020-8{_}997{_}The view here shown is looking into the small dining-room, the private dining-room of their Majesties.
10020-8{_}998{_}In it there is to be seen a costly collection of miniatures, nearly a hundred and twenty in number, every one of them from the hand of Dutch masters.
10020-8{_}999{_}They are all beautifully framed in groups.
10020-8{_}1000{_}In the photograph you will observe a finely carved side-board with some of these miniatures showing on either side.
10020-8{_}1001{_}Also in this room you will find several specimens of engraving on brass and some Russian productions in malachite.
10020-8{_}1002{_}[Illustration: THE STATE BALL-ROOM.
10020-8{_}1003{_}_From a Photo by Gunn & Stuart, Richmond._]  Now to the State ball-room--a nobly proportioned room, but of somewhat severe aspect Some good relief carving is shown and a splendid parquetry floor; also some costly furniture, over-burnished and upholstered in crimson with floral devices.
10020-8{_}1004{_}No doubt it has a very imposing and gay appearance when lighted up and filled with guests.
10020-8{_}1005{_}Nearly seven hundred lights are displayed, which would naturally cause a most brilliant effect.
10020-8{_}1006{_}Somehow ball-rooms are never satisfactory when viewed in the day-time, unless you have an eye for proportions only; in that case this one could not fail to please, as it cannot be less than 90ft.
10020-8{_}1007{_}long and is of magnificent height, added to by a glass concave roof.
10020-8{_}1008{_}[Illustration: THE QUEEN'S RECEPTION-ROOM.
10020-8{_}1009{_}_From a Photo by Gunn & Stuart, Richmond._]  The Queen's reception-room is prettily hung in crimson with designs depicting art and music; the furniture bright and handsome in crimson and cream.
10020-8{_}1010{_}On either side of the fireplace stand some crimson velvet screens in burnished frames, the crown and arms worked on the velvet in characters of gold.
10020-8{_}1011{_}In the accompanying view you will observe a large album on a stand; this was given to the Queen-Regent by the ladies of Holland.
10020-8{_}1012{_}It is of leather, with ormolu mounts, on the covers being painted panels and flowers worked in silk, these flowers being surrounded with rubies and pearls; and at either corner is a large sapphire.
10020-8{_}1013{_}The interior shows pages of vellum, with names of subscribers beautifully inscribed.
10020-8{_}1014{_}This room will, of course, be the one where the young Queen will receive when she commences to reign.
10020-8{_}1015{_}From here I went to view a suite of apartments, formerly the property of Queen Sophia, the first Consort of the late King.
10020-8{_}1016{_}These rooms are still in the same condition as when Her Majesty died; they are very fine rooms, and contain a vast number of curios of every description.
10020-8{_}1017{_}They are lined entirely from floor to ceiling with mahogany; the furniture, which is massive, antique, and beautifully carved, being also of mahogany and tulip wood.
10020-8{_}1018{_}I find one of Erard's grand pianos standing in the boudoir, and am told that it was a favourite instrument of the late Queen.
10020-8{_}1019{_}There are some fine specimens of vases: one an "Adam and Eve," some of Swiss make, and others of Dresden.
10020-8{_}1020{_}Also I note an exquisite model of a ship, an inlaid Empire mirror, and other treasures too numerous to particularize.
10020-8{_}1021{_}[Illustration: OVER-MANTEL IN TEA-ROOM.
10020-8{_}1022{_}_From a Photo by Gunn & Stuart, Richmond._]  The tea-room is another that I must make brief mention of.
10020-8{_}1023{_}It contains some valuable souvenirs in the form of vases, some from the Emperor Napoleon (these are jewelled), some from William IV.
10020-8{_}1024{_}of Germany, and some from the Emperor Frederick.
10020-8{_}1025{_}Then there are others from Berlin and Potsdam, and still others of Svres.
10020-8{_}1026{_}On the marble mantel is a very intricate French timepiece, and over it an exquisite silver-framed mirror.
10020-8{_}1027{_}An inlaid mosaic table is a feature here.
10020-8{_}1028{_}The worth of it must be fabulous; the design is marvellously executed.
10020-8{_}1029{_}Pope Pius IX.
10020-8{_}1030{_}was the donor.
10020-8{_}1031{_}This room is really the tea-room for the Royal ladies when in residence.
10020-8{_}1032{_}Music is again to the fore, and here Steinway is the favourite, one of his grand pianos occupying the place of honour.
10020-8{_}1033{_}Now I go downstairs for a brief survey of the private apartments of the late King.
10020-8{_}1034{_}I shall not attempt to describe them in detail, but content myself with mention of one or two things I specially noticed.
10020-8{_}1035{_}I started with the billiard-room, a good-sized room and well fitted; but obscured by the covers denoting non-usage.
10020-8{_}1036{_}One curious article I must note.
10020-8{_}1037{_}It is a clock and musical-box combined, giving out a variety of twenty-seven tunes.
10020-8{_}1038{_}The visible part of it is a pure alabaster representation of the tomb of our Henry II, supported by lions couchant.
10020-8{_}1039{_}Rather a strange model for a musical-box containing lively airs, is it not?
10020-8{_}1040{_}[Illustration: THE LATE KING'S SITTING-ROOM.
10020-8{_}1041{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}1042{_}by Gunn & Stuart, Richmond._]  Then I pass on through the King's dining-room, a stately and richly-appointed apartment.
10020-8{_}1043{_}On through the Ministers' room, and so into His Majesty's private sitting-room.
10020-8{_}1044{_}Here I cannot but linger, there are so many treasures rich and rare, the chief of which consists in the elaborate cabinets and other furniture, all of tortoiseshell and silver, quite the best I have seen of its kind.
10020-8{_}1045{_}Some of it looks as though crammed with secret drawers, and I stand before it wondering whether Queen Wilhelmina will be as anxious to discover and overhaul them as _I_ should be.
10020-8{_}1046{_}I could tell you a deal more of what I saw at this Palace at Den Haag, but, doubtless, have said enough to show you something of its wealth of appointments and costly treasures.
10020-8{_}1047{_}One cannot help thinking what a sum all this has cost, and what it must take to keep up so many places; but the Royal Family of the Netherlands have well-lined coffers, as it is not only their own country that owns their supremacy, but they have also many dependencies in the Indies, bringing in enormous revenues.
10020-8{_}1048{_}[Illustration: "T'HUIS IN'T BOSCH," NEAR DEN HAAG.
10020-8{_}1049{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}1050{_}by Gunn & Stuart, Richmond._]  I have mentioned three Palaces; I know of five; but will close with just a few words respecting a fourth, and a view of the same, which is charmingly pretty.
10020-8{_}1051{_}This Palace is called "T'Huis in't Bosch," and is just a nice carriage drive from the town of Den Haag.
10020-8{_}1052{_}It stands right in the midst of a beautiful park, with herds of deer and hundreds of gay-plumaged birds--a park that far and away surpasses even our vaunted Richmond Park--magnificent timber, dense undergrowth, wild flowers in profusion, and now and again winding lakes and streams, crossed by rustic bridges, and such views over hill and dale as would delight either an artist or an admirer of Nature.
10020-8{_}1053{_}The above view of the house will give a good idea of its outside appearance.
10020-8{_}1054{_}I have no time for interiors, or should be tempted to prolong this indefinitely.
10020-8{_}1055{_}We have had a peep at the Palaces of Holland, and many of us will know more of the country and its reigning family for the visit.
10020-8{_}1056{_}Holland, with its youthful Queen, has a future we cannot wot of, but we all hope it is a prosperous and bright one, and we all agree in thanking Her Majesty the Queen-Regent for the opportunity of gaining this information, and wish for her daughter all the happiness and wisdom that she--the Royal mother--could desire for her.
10020-8{_}1057{_}*       *       *       *       *  [_The Illustrated Interviews will be continued as usual next month_.]
10020-8{_}1058{_}*       *       *       *       *    _Zig-Zags at the Zoo._  XIX.
10020-8{_}1059{_}ZIG-ZAG BATRACHIAN.
10020-8{_}1060{_}[Illustration]   The frog and the toad suffer, in this world of injustice, from a deprival of the respect and esteem that is certainly their due.
10020-8{_}1061{_}In the case of the frog this may be due largely to the animal's headlong and harlequin-like character, but the toad is a steady personage, whose solemnity of deportment, not to speak of his stoutness, entitles him to high consideration in a world where grave dulness and personal circumference always attract reverence.
10020-8{_}1062{_}The opening lines of a certain famous poem have without a doubt done much to damage the dignity of the frog.
10020-8{_}1063{_}"The frog he would a-wooing go" is not, perhaps, disrespectful, although flippant; but "whether his mother would let him or no" is a gross insult.
10020-8{_}1064{_}Of course, it is a matter upon which no self-respecting frog ever consults his mother; but the absurd jingle is immortal, and the frog's dignity suffers by it.
10020-8{_}1065{_}Then there is a certain pot-bellied smugness of appearance about the frog that provokes a smile in the irreverent.
10020-8{_}1066{_}Still, the frog has received some consideration in his time.
10020-8{_}1067{_}The great Homer himself did not disdain to sing the mighty battle of the frogs and mice; and Aristophanes gave the frogs a most important chorus in one of his comedies; moreover, calling the whole comedy "The Frogs," although he had his choice of title-names among many very notable characters--schylus, Euripides, Bacchus, Pluto, Proserpine, and other leaders of society.
10020-8{_}1068{_}Still, in every way the frog and the toad are underesteemed--as though such a thing as a worthy family frog or an honourable toad of business were in Nature impossible.
10020-8{_}1069{_}It is not as though they were useless.
10020-8{_}1070{_}The frog's hind legs make an excellent dish for those who like it, as well as a joke for those who don't.
10020-8{_}1071{_}Powdered toad held in the palm is a fine thing to stop the nose bleeding--or, at any rate, it was a couple of hundred years ago, according to a dear old almanac I have.
10020-8{_}1072{_}On the same unimpeachable authority I may fearlessly affirm a smashed frog--smashed on the proper saint's day--in conjunction with hair taken from a ram's forehead and a nail stolen from a piebald mare's shoe, to be a certain remedy for ague, worn in a little leather bag.
10020-8{_}1073{_}If it fails it will be because the moon was in the wrong quarter, or the mare was not sufficiently piebald, or the nail was not stolen with sufficient dishonesty, or some mistake of that sort.
10020-8{_}1074{_}[Illustration: A SMALL LUNCH.]
10020-8{_}1075{_}Personally, I am rather fond of frogs and toads.
10020-8{_}1076{_}This, of course, in a strictly platonic sense, and entirely apart from dinner.
10020-8{_}1077{_}A toad I admire even more than a frog, because of his gentlemanly calm.
10020-8{_}1078{_}He never rushes at his food ravenously, as do so many other creatures.
10020-8{_}1079{_}Place a worm near him and you will see.
10020-8{_}1080{_}He inspects the worm casually, first with one eye and then with the other, as who would say: "Luncheon?
10020-8{_}1081{_}Certainly.
10020-8{_}1082{_}Delighted, I'm sure."
10020-8{_}1083{_}Then he sits placidly awhile, as though thinking of something else altogether.
10020-8{_}1084{_}Presently he rises slightly on his feet and looks a little--very little--more attentively at the worm.
10020-8{_}1085{_}"Oh, yes," he is saying--"luncheon, of course.
10020-8{_}1086{_}Whenever you like, you know."
10020-8{_}1087{_}And he becomes placid again, as though interested in the general conversation.
10020-8{_}1088{_}After a little he suddenly straightens his hind legs and bends down over the worm, like a man saying, "Ah, and what have we got here now?
10020-8{_}1089{_}Oh, worm--_ver au naturel_--capital, capital!"
10020-8{_}1090{_}After this there is nothing to do but to eat, and this the toad does without the smallest delay.
10020-8{_}1091{_}For leisurely indifference, followed by a business-like grab, nothing can beat a toad.
10020-8{_}1092{_}Almost before the cover is lifted, figuratively speaking, the worm's head and tail are wriggling, like a lively moustache, out of the sides of the toad's mouth.
10020-8{_}1093{_}The head and tail he gently pats in with his hands, and there is no longer any worm; after which the toad smiles affably and comfortably, possibly meditating a liqueur.
10020-8{_}1094{_}I have an especial regard for the giant toad in one of the cases against the inner wall of the reptile-house lobby.
10020-8{_}1095{_}There is a pimpliness of countenance and a comfortable capaciousness of waistcoat about him that always make me wonder what he has done with his churchwarden and pewter.
10020-8{_}1096{_}He has a serene, confidential, well-old-pal-how-are-you way of regarding Tyrrell, his keeper.
10020-8{_}1097{_}Of late (for some few months, that is) the giant toad has been turning something over in his mind, as one may perceive from his cogitative demeanour.
10020-8{_}1098{_}He is thinking, I am convinced, of the new Goliath Beetle.
10020-8{_}1099{_}The Goliath Beetle, he is thinking, would make rather a fit supper for the Giant Toad.
10020-8{_}1100{_}This because he has never seen the beetle.
10020-8{_}1101{_}His mind might be set at rest by an introduction to Goliath, but the acquaintanceship would do no good to the beetle's morals.
10020-8{_}1102{_}At present Goliath is a most exemplary vegetarian and tea-drinker, but evil communications with that pimply, dissipated toad would wreck his principles.
10020-8{_}1103{_}[Illustration: "THINK I COULD MANAGE THAT BEETLE, TYRRELL?"]
10020-8{_}1104{_}[Illustration: EVIL COMMUNICATIONS.]
10020-8{_}1105{_}Why one should speak of the Adorned Ceratophrys when the thing might just as well be called the Barking Frog, I don't know.
10020-8{_}1106{_}Let us compromise and call him the Adorned C., in the manner of Mr. Wemmick.
10020-8{_}1107{_}I respect the Adorned C. almost as much as if he were a toad instead of a frog, but chiefly I admire his mouth.
10020-8{_}1108{_}A crocodile has a very respectable mouth--when it separates its jaws it opens its head.
10020-8{_}1109{_}But when the Adorned C. smiles he opens out his entire anatomical bag of tricks-- comes as near bisecting himself indeed as may be; opens, in short, like a Gladstone bag.
10020-8{_}1110{_}From a fat person, of course, you expect a broad, genial smile; but you are doubly gratified when you find it extending all round him.
10020-8{_}1111{_}That, you feel, is indeed no end of a smile--and that is the smile of the Adorned C.  [Illustration] [Illustration: "DON'T SQUEEZE SO, TYRRELL!"]
10020-8{_}1112{_}[Illustration: "WANT ME TO BARK?"]
10020-8{_}1113{_}[Illustration: "HE CALLS THIS WINDING ME UP!"]
10020-8{_}1114{_}[Illustration: "SHAN'T BARK--"] [Illustration: "SO THERE!"]
10020-8{_}1115{_}[Illustration: "STOW THAT, TYRRELL!"]
10020-8{_}1116{_}[Illustration: "HE'S ALWAYS DOING THAT."]
10020-8{_}1117{_}[Illustration: "I'LL GET SO WILD IN A MINUTE!"]
10020-8{_}1118{_}[Illustration: "GUR-R-R-R-."]
10020-8{_}1119{_}[Illustration: "WOW, WOW!"]
10020-8{_}1120{_}[Illustration: "SNAP!
10020-8{_}1121{_}WOW-WOW!"]
10020-8{_}1122{_}[Illustration: "WHAT, GOT TO GO BACK?"]
10020-8{_}1123{_}[Illustration: "GOOD NIGHT.
10020-8{_}1124{_}TYRRELL!"]
10020-8{_}1125{_}But, notwithstanding this smile, the Adorned C. is short of temper.
10020-8{_}1126{_}Indeed, you may only make him bark by practising upon this fact.
10020-8{_}1127{_}Tyrrell's private performance with the Adorned C. is one that irresistibly reminds the spectator of Lieutenant Cole's with his figures, and would scarcely be improved by ventriloquism itself.
10020-8{_}1128{_}The Adorned C. prefers biting to barking, and his bite is worse than his bark--bites always are, except in the proverb.
10020-8{_}1129{_}This is why Tyrrell holds the Adorned C. pretty tight whenever he touches him.
10020-8{_}1130{_}The one aspiration of the Adorned C. is for a quiet life, and he defends his aspiration with bites and barks.
10020-8{_}1131{_}Tyrrell touches him gently, cautiously, and repeatedly on the back until the annoyance is no longer to be tolerated, and then the Adorned C. duly barks like a terrier.
10020-8{_}1132{_}Now, the most interesting thing about the Adorned C., after his mouth, is his bark, and why he should be reluctant to exhibit it except under pressure of irritation--why he should hide his light under a bushel of ill-temper--I can't conceive.
10020-8{_}1133{_}It is as though Patti wouldn't sing till her manager threw an egg at her, or as though Sir Frederick Leighton would only paint a picture after Mr. Whistler had broken his studio windows with a brick.
10020-8{_}1134{_}Even the whistling oyster of London tradition would perform without requiring a preliminary insult or personal assault.
10020-8{_}1135{_}But let us account everything good if possible; perhaps the Adorned C. only suffers from a modest dislike for vain display; although this is scarcely consistent with the internal exhibition afforded by his smile.
10020-8{_}1136{_}[Illustration] [Illustration] [Illustration] [Illustration] [Illustration] [Illustration] [Illustration] [Illustration] [Illustration]  With the distinction of residence in the main court of the reptile-house itself, as also with the knowledge of its rarity, the Smooth-clawed Frog sets no small value on himself.
10020-8{_}1137{_}He lives in water perpetually, and is always bobbing mysteriously about in it with his four-fingered hands spread out before him.
10020-8{_}1138{_}This seems to me to be nothing but a vulgar manifestation of the Smooth-clawed Frog's self-appreciation.
10020-8{_}1139{_}He is like a coster conducting a Dutch auction, except that it is himself that he puts up for the bids of admiring visitors.
10020-8{_}1140{_}With his double bunch of four fingers held eagerly before him he says--or means to say--"'Ere--eight!
10020-8{_}1141{_}Ain't that cheap enough?
10020-8{_}1142{_}Eight!
10020-8{_}1143{_}Going at eight.
10020-8{_}1144{_}Who says eight?
10020-8{_}1145{_}Now then--eight; for a noble frog like me!"
10020-8{_}1146{_}Presently, he wriggles a little in the water, as though vexed at the slackness of offers; then he drops one of the hands and leaves the other outstretched.
10020-8{_}1147{_}"'Ere--four!
10020-8{_}1148{_}Anythink to do business.
10020-8{_}1149{_}Four!
10020-8{_}1150{_}Nobody say four?
10020-8{_}1151{_}Oh, blow this!"
10020-8{_}1152{_}and with a jerk of one long paddle he dives among the weeds.
10020-8{_}1153{_}"Them shiny-lookin' swells ain't got no money!"
10020-8{_}1154{_}is what I am convinced he reports to his friends.
10020-8{_}1155{_}The Smooth-clawed Frog has lately begun to breed here, a thing before unknown; so that his rarity and value are in danger of depreciation.
10020-8{_}1156{_}But such is his inordinate conceit of himself that I am convinced he will always begin the bidding with eight.
10020-8{_}1157{_}[Illustration] [Illustration] [Illustration: "HAPPY?"]
10020-8{_}1158{_}[Illustration: "I AM HAPPY."]
10020-8{_}1159{_}[Illustration: "WHY SHOULDN'T I BE HAPPY?"]
10020-8{_}1160{_}[Illustration: "THE SOCIETY LODGES ME."]
10020-8{_}1161{_}[Illustration: "TYRRELL FEEDS ME."]
10020-8{_}1162{_}[Illustration: "NO EXPENSE TO ME, YOU KNOW."]
10020-8{_}1163{_}[Illustration] [Illustration: "GOOD DAY TO YOU."]
10020-8{_}1164{_}If you rejoice in the sight of a really happy, contented frog, you should stand long before White's Green Frog, and study his smile.
10020-8{_}1165{_}No other frog has a smile like this; some are wider, perhaps, but that is nothing.
10020-8{_}1166{_}A frog is ordained by Nature to smile much, but the smile seems commonly one of hunger merely, though often one of stomach-ache.
10020-8{_}1167{_}White's Green Frog smiles broad content and placid felicity.
10020-8{_}1168{_}Maintained in comfort, with no necessity to earn his living, this is probably natural; still, the bison enjoys the same advantages, although nobody ever saw him smile; but, then, an animal soon to become extinct can scarcely be expected to smile.
10020-8{_}1169{_}In the smile of White's Green Frog, however, I fear, a certain smug, Pecksniffian quality is visible.
10020-8{_}1170{_}"I am a Numble individual, my Christian friends," he seems to say, "and my wants, which are few and simple, are providentially supplied.
10020-8{_}1171{_}Therefore, I am Truly Happy.
10020-8{_}1172{_}It is no great merit in my merely batrachian nature that I am Truly Happy; a cheerful countenance, my friends, is a duty imposed on me by an indulgent Providence."
10020-8{_}1173{_}White's Green Frog may, however, be in reality a frog of excellent moral worth: and I trust that Green's White Frog, if ever he is discovered, will be a moral frog too.
10020-8{_}1174{_}[Illustration: "HERE WE ARE!"]
10020-8{_}1175{_}[Illustration: "HOW DO?
10020-8{_}1176{_}I'M OFF."]
10020-8{_}1177{_}[Illustration: "EH?"]
10020-8{_}1178{_}[Illustration: "WHAT?"]
10020-8{_}1179{_}[Illustration: "WHO'S THAT?"]
10020-8{_}1180{_}[Illustration]  By-the-bye, some green frogs are blue.
10020-8{_}1181{_}That is to say, individuals of the green species have been found of the skyey colour and sold at a good price as rarities.
10020-8{_}1182{_}When it was not easy to find one already blue, the prudent tradesman kept a green frog in a blue glass vase for a few weeks, and brought it out as blue as you might wish.
10020-8{_}1183{_}The colour stayed long enough, as a rule, to admit of sale at a decent price, but was liable to fade after.
10020-8{_}1184{_}As I think I have said, the toad is distinguished by a placid calm denied to the frog; therefore it is singular that the ordinary toad's Latin name should be _Bufo vulgaris_--a name suggestive of nothing so much as a low--disgracefully low--comedian.
10020-8{_}1185{_}_Bufo vulgaris_ should be the name of a very inferior, rowdy clown.
10020-8{_}1186{_}The frog is a much nearer approximation to this character than the toad.
10020-8{_}1187{_}The frog comes headlong with a bound, a bunch of legs and arms, with his "Here we are again!
10020-8{_}1188{_}Fine day to-morrow, wasn't it?"
10020-8{_}1189{_}and goes off with another bound, before the toad, who is gravely analyzing the metaphysical aspect of nothing in particular, can open his eyes to look up.
10020-8{_}1190{_}The toad has one comic act, however, of infinitely greater humour than the bouncing buffooneries of the frog.
10020-8{_}1191{_}When the toad casts his skin he quietly rolls it up over his back and head, just as a man skins off a close-fitting jersey.
10020-8{_}1192{_}Once having drawn it well over his nose, however, he immediately proceeds to cram it down his throat with both hands, and so it finally disappears.
10020-8{_}1193{_}Now, this is a performance of genuine and grotesque humour, which it is worth keeping a toad to see.
10020-8{_}1194{_}*       *       *       *       *    _The Helmet._  From the French by Ferdinand Beissier.
10020-8{_}1195{_}[Illustration]   "But, uncle--I love my cousin!"
10020-8{_}1196{_}"Get out!"
10020-8{_}1197{_}"Give her to me."
10020-8{_}1198{_}"Don't bother me!"
10020-8{_}1199{_}"It will be my death!"
10020-8{_}1200{_}"Nonsense!
10020-8{_}1201{_}you'll console yourself with some other girl."
10020-8{_}1202{_}"Pray--"  My uncle, whose back had been towards me, whirled round, his face red to bursting, and brought his closed fist down upon the counter with a heavy thump.
10020-8{_}1203{_}"Never!"
10020-8{_}1204{_}he cried; "never: Do you hear what I say?"
10020-8{_}1205{_}And as I looked at him beseechingly and with joined hands, he went on:--  "A pretty husband you look like!--without a sou, and dreaming of going into housekeeping!
10020-8{_}1206{_}A nice mess I should make of it, by giving you my daughter!
10020-8{_}1207{_}It's no use your insisting.
10020-8{_}1208{_}You know that when I have said 'No,' nothing under the sun can make me say 'Yes'!"
10020-8{_}1209{_}I ceased to make any further appeal.
10020-8{_}1210{_}I knew my uncle--about as headstrong an old fellow as could be found in a day's search.
10020-8{_}1211{_}I contented myself with giving vent to a deep sigh, and then went on with the furbishing of a big, double-handed sword, rusty from point to hilt.
10020-8{_}1212{_}This memorable conversation took place, in fact, in the shop of my maternal uncle, a well-known dealer in antiquities and _objets d'art_, No.
10020-8{_}1213{_}53, Rue des Claquettes, at the sign of the "Maltese Cross"--a perfect museum of curiosities.
10020-8{_}1214{_}The walls were hung with Marseilles and old Rouen china, facing ancient cuirasses, sabres, and muskets, and picture frames; below these were ranged old cabinets, coffers of all sorts, and statues of saints, one-armed or one-legged for the most part and dilapidated as to their gilding; then, here and there, in glass cases, hermetically closed and locked, there were knick-knacks in infinite variety--lachrymatories, tiny urns, rings, precious stones, fragments of marble, bracelets, crosses, necklaces, medals, and miniature ivory statuettes, the yellow tints of which, in the sun, took momentarily a flesh-like transparency.
10020-8{_}1215{_}Time out of mind the shop had belonged to the Cornuberts.
10020-8{_}1216{_}It passed regularly from father to son, and my uncle--his neighbours said--could not but be the possessor of a nice little fortune.
10020-8{_}1217{_}Held in esteem by all, a Municipal Councillor, impressed by the importance and gravity of his office, short, fat, highly choleric and headstrong, but at bottom not in the least degree an unkind sort of man--such was my uncle Cornubert, my only living male relative, who, as soon as I left school, had elevated me to the dignity of chief and only clerk and shopman of the "Maltese Cross."
10020-8{_}1218{_}But my uncle was not only a dealer in antiquities and a Municipal Councillor, he was yet more, and above all, the father of my cousin Rose, with whom I was naturally in love.
10020-8{_}1219{_}To come back to the point at which I digressed.
10020-8{_}1220{_}Without paying any attention to the sighs which exhaled from my bosom while scouring the rust from my long, two-handed sword, my uncle, magnifying glass in hand, was engaged in the examination of a lot of medals which he had purchased that morning.
10020-8{_}1221{_}Suddenly he raised his head; five o'clock was striking.
10020-8{_}1222{_}"The Council!"
10020-8{_}1223{_}he cried.
10020-8{_}1224{_}When my uncle pronounced that august word, it made a mouthful; for a pin, he would have saluted it bare-headed.
10020-8{_}1225{_}But, this time, after a moment's consideration, he tapped his forehead and added, in a tone of supreme relief:--  "No, the sitting does not take place before to-morrow--and I am forgetting that I have to go to the railway station to get the consignment of which I was advised this morning."
10020-8{_}1226{_}Rising from his seat, and laying down his glass, he called out:--  "Rose, give me my cane and hat!"
10020-8{_}1227{_}Then, turning towards me, he added, in a lowered tone and speaking very quickly:--  "As to you--don't forget our conversation.
10020-8{_}1228{_}If you think you can make me say 'yes,' try!--but I don't think you'll succeed.
10020-8{_}1229{_}Meanwhile, not a word to Rose, or, by Saint Barthlemy, my patron of happy memory, I'll instantly kick you out of doors!"
10020-8{_}1230{_}[Illustration: "AT THAT MOMENT ROSE APPEARED."]
10020-8{_}1231{_}At that moment Rose appeared with my uncle's cane and hat, which she handed to him.
10020-8{_}1232{_}He kissed her on the forehead; then, giving me a last but eloquent look, hurried from the shop.
10020-8{_}1233{_}I went on scouring my double-handed sword.
10020-8{_}1234{_}Rose came quietly towards me.
10020-8{_}1235{_}"What is the matter with my father?"
10020-8{_}1236{_}she asked; "he seems to be angry with you."
10020-8{_}1237{_}I looked at her--her eyes were so black, her look so kind, her mouth so rosy, and her teeth so white that I told her all--my love, my suit to her father, and his rough refusal.
10020-8{_}1238{_}I could not help it--after all, it was _his_ fault!
10020-8{_}1239{_}He was not there: I determined to brave his anger.
10020-8{_}1240{_}Besides, there is nobody like timid persons for displaying courage under certain circumstances.
10020-8{_}1241{_}My cousin said nothing; she only held down her eyes--while her cheeks were as red as those of cherries in May.
10020-8{_}1242{_}I checked myself.
10020-8{_}1243{_}"Are you angry with me?"
10020-8{_}1244{_}I asked, tremblingly.
10020-8{_}1245{_}"Are you angry with me, Rose?"
10020-8{_}1246{_}She held out to me her hand.
10020-8{_}1247{_}On that, my heart seething with audacity, my head on fire, I cried:--  "Rose--I swear it!
10020-8{_}1248{_}I will be your husband!"
10020-8{_}1249{_}And as she shook her head and looked at me sadly, I added: "Oh!
10020-8{_}1250{_}I well know that my uncle is self-willed, but I will be more self-willed still; and, since he must be forced to say 'yes,' I will force him to say it!"
10020-8{_}1251{_}"But how?"
10020-8{_}1252{_}asked Rose.
10020-8{_}1253{_}Ah!
10020-8{_}1254{_}how?
10020-8{_}1255{_}That was exactly the difficulty.
10020-8{_}1256{_}But, no matter; I would find a way to surmount it!
10020-8{_}1257{_}At that moment a heavy step resounded in the street.
10020-8{_}1258{_}Instinctively we moved away from each other; I returned to my double-handed sword, and Rose, to keep herself in countenance, set to dusting, with a corner of her apron, a little statuette in its faded red velvet case.
10020-8{_}1259{_}My uncle entered.
10020-8{_}1260{_}Surprised at finding us together, he stopped short and looked sharply at us, from one to the other.
10020-8{_}1261{_}We each of us went on rubbing without raising our heads.
10020-8{_}1262{_}"Here, take this," said my uncle, handing me a bulky parcel from under his arm.
10020-8{_}1263{_}"A splendid purchase, you'll see."
10020-8{_}1264{_}The subject did not interest me in the least.
10020-8{_}1265{_}I opened the parcel, and from the enveloping paper emerged a steel helmet--but not an ordinary helmet, oh, no!--a superb, a monumental morion, with gorget and pointed visor of strange form.
10020-8{_}1266{_}The visor was raised, and I tried to discover what prevented it from being lowered.
10020-8{_}1267{_}"It will not go down--the hinges have got out of order," said my uncle; "but it's a superb piece, and, when it has been thoroughly cleaned and touched up, will look well--that shall be your to-morrow's job."
10020-8{_}1268{_}"Very good, uncle," I murmured, not daring to raise my eyes to his.
10020-8{_}1269{_}That night, on reaching my room, I at once went to bed.
10020-8{_}1270{_}I was eager to be alone and able to think at my ease.
10020-8{_}1271{_}Night brings counsel, it is said; and I had great need that the proverb should prove true.
10020-8{_}1272{_}But, after lying awake for an hour without receiving any assistance, I fell off to sleep, and, till next morning, did nothing but dream the oddest dreams.
10020-8{_}1273{_}I saw Rose on her way to church in a strange bridal costume, a 14th-century cap, three feet high, on her head, but looking prettier than ever; then suddenly the scene changed to moonlight, in which innumerable helmets and pieces of old china were dancing a wild farandola, while my uncle, clad in complete armour and with a formidable halberd in his hand, conducted the bewildering whirl.
10020-8{_}1274{_}[Illustration: "MY UNCLE SAT SMOKING HIS PIPE AND WATCHING ME."]
10020-8{_}1275{_}The next day--ah, the next day!--I was no nearer.
10020-8{_}1276{_}In vain, with clenched teeth, I scoured the immense helmet brought by my uncle the previous evening--scoured it with such fury as almost to break the iron; not an idea came to me.
10020-8{_}1277{_}The helmet shone like a sun: my uncle sat smoking his pipe and watching me; but I could think of nothing, of no way of forcing him to give me his daughter.
10020-8{_}1278{_}At three o'clock Rose went into the country, whence she was not to return until dinner-time, in the evening.
10020-8{_}1279{_}On the threshold she could only make a sign to me with her hand; my uncle had not left us alone for a single instant.
10020-8{_}1280{_}He was not easy in his mind; I could see that by his face.
10020-8{_}1281{_}No doubt he had not forgotten our conversation of the previous evening.
10020-8{_}1282{_}I went on rubbing at my helmet.
10020-8{_}1283{_}"You have made it quite bright enough--put it down," said my uncle.
10020-8{_}1284{_}I put it down.
10020-8{_}1285{_}The storm was gathering: I could not do better than allow it to blow over.
10020-8{_}1286{_}But suddenly, as if overtaken by a strange fancy, my uncle took up the enormous morion and turned and examined it on all sides.
10020-8{_}1287{_}"A handsome piece of armour, there is no doubt about it; but it must have weighed pretty heavily on its wearer's shoulders," he muttered; and, urged by I know not what demon, he clapped it on his head and latched the gorget-piece about his neck.
10020-8{_}1288{_}Struck almost speechless, I watched what he was doing--thinking only how ugly he looked.
10020-8{_}1289{_}Suddenly there was a sharp sound--as if a spring had snapped--and--crack!--down fell the visor; and there was my uncle, with his head in an iron cage, gesticulating and swearing like a pagan!
10020-8{_}1290{_}I could contain myself no longer, and burst into a roar of laughter; for my uncle, stumpy, fat, and rubicund, presented an irresistibly comic appearance.
10020-8{_}1291{_}[Illustration: "THREATENINGLY HE CAME TOWARDS ME."]
10020-8{_}1292{_}Threateningly, he came towards me.
10020-8{_}1293{_}"The hinges!--the hinges, fool!"
10020-8{_}1294{_}he yelled.
10020-8{_}1295{_}I could not see his face, but I felt that it was red to bursting.
10020-8{_}1296{_}"When you have done laughing, idiot!"
10020-8{_}1297{_}he cried.
10020-8{_}1298{_}But the helmet swayed so oddly on his shoulders, his voice came from out it in such strange tones, that the more he gesticulated, the more he yelled and threatened me, the louder I laughed.
10020-8{_}1299{_}At that moment the clock of the Htel-de-Ville, striking five, was heard.
10020-8{_}1300{_}"The Municipal Council!"
10020-8{_}1301{_}murmured my uncle, in a stifled voice.
10020-8{_}1302{_}"Quick!
10020-8{_}1303{_}help me off with this beast of a machine!
10020-8{_}1304{_}We'll settle our business afterwards!"
10020-8{_}1305{_}But, suddenly likewise, an idea--a wild, extraordinary idea--came into my head; but then, whoever is madder than a lover?
10020-8{_}1306{_}Besides, I had no choice of means.
10020-8{_}1307{_}"No!"
10020-8{_}1308{_}I replied.
10020-8{_}1309{_}My uncle fell back two paces in terror--and again the enormous helmet wobbled on his shoulders.
10020-8{_}1310{_}"No," I repeated, firmly, "I'll not help you out, unless you give me the hand of my cousin Rose!"
10020-8{_}1311{_}From the depths of the strangely elongated visor came, not an angry exclamation, but a veritable roar.
10020-8{_}1312{_}I had "done it!"
10020-8{_}1313{_}--I had burned my ships!
10020-8{_}1314{_}"If you do not consent to do what I ask of you," I added, "not only will I not help you off with your helmet, but I will call in all your neighbours, and then go and find the Municipal Council!"
10020-8{_}1315{_}"You'll end your days on the scaffold!"
10020-8{_}1316{_}cried my uncle.
10020-8{_}1317{_}"The hand of Rose!"
10020-8{_}1318{_}I repeated.
10020-8{_}1319{_}"You told me that it would only be by force that you would be made to say 'yes'--say it, or I will call in the neighbours!"
10020-8{_}1320{_}The clock was still striking; my uncle raised his arms as if to curse me.
10020-8{_}1321{_}"Decide at once," I cried, "somebody is coming!"
10020-8{_}1322{_}"Well, then--yes!"
10020-8{_}1323{_}murmured my uncle.
10020-8{_}1324{_}"But make haste!"
10020-8{_}1325{_}"On your word of honour?"
10020-8{_}1326{_}"On my word of honour!"
10020-8{_}1327{_}The visor gave way, the gorget-piece also, and my uncle's head issued from durance, red as a poppy.
10020-8{_}1328{_}Just in time.
10020-8{_}1329{_}The chemist at the corner, a colleague in the Municipal Council, entered the shop.
10020-8{_}1330{_}"Are you coming?"
10020-8{_}1331{_}he asked; "they will be beginning the business without us."
10020-8{_}1332{_}"I'm coming," replied my uncle.
10020-8{_}1333{_}And without looking at me, he took up his hat and cane and hurried out.
10020-8{_}1334{_}The next moment all my hopes had vanished.
10020-8{_}1335{_}My uncle would surely not forgive me.
10020-8{_}1336{_}At dinner-time I took my place at table on his right hand in low spirits, ate little, and said nothing.
10020-8{_}1337{_}"It will come with the dessert," I thought.
10020-8{_}1338{_}Rose looked at me, and I avoided meeting her eyes.
10020-8{_}1339{_}As I had expected, the dessert over, my uncle lit his pipe, raised his head, and then--  "Rose--come here!"
10020-8{_}1340{_}Rose went to him.
10020-8{_}1341{_}"Do you know what that fellow there asked me to do, yesterday?"
10020-8{_}1342{_}I trembled like a leaf, and Rose did the same.
10020-8{_}1343{_}[Illustration: "DO YOU LOVE HIM?"]
10020-8{_}1344{_}"To give him your hand," he added.
10020-8{_}1345{_}"Do you love him?"
10020-8{_}1346{_}Rose cast down her eyes.
10020-8{_}1347{_}"Very well," continued my uncle; "on this side, the case is complete.
10020-8{_}1348{_}Come here, you."
10020-8{_}1349{_}I approached him.
10020-8{_}1350{_}"Here I am, uncle," and, in a whisper.
10020-8{_}1351{_}I added quickly: "Forgive me!"
10020-8{_}1352{_}He burst into a hearty laugh.
10020-8{_}1353{_}"Marry her, then, donkey--since you love her, and I give her to you!"
10020-8{_}1354{_}"Ah!--uncle!"
10020-8{_}1355{_}"Ah!--dear papa!"
10020-8{_}1356{_}And Rose and I threw ourselves into his arms.
10020-8{_}1357{_}"Very good!
10020-8{_}1358{_}very good!"
10020-8{_}1359{_}he cried, wiping his eyes.
10020-8{_}1360{_}"Be happy, that's all I ask."
10020-8{_}1361{_}And, in turn, he whispered in my ear:--  "I should have given her to you all the same, you big goose; but--keep the story of the helmet between us two!"
10020-8{_}1362{_}I give you my word that I have never told it but to Rose, my dear little wife.
10020-8{_}1363{_}And, if ever you pass along the Rue des Claquettes, No.
10020-8{_}1364{_}53, at the place of honour in the old shop, I'll show you my uncle's helmet, which we would never sell.
10020-8{_}1365{_}*       *       *       *       *    _The Music of Nature._  BY A. T. CAMDEN PRATT.
10020-8{_}1366{_}II.
10020-8{_}1367{_}Reference was made at the close of the last article to the voice of the dog, and his method of making his feelings and desires understood.
10020-8{_}1368{_}It is, of course, well known that this is an acquired habit, or accomplishment.
10020-8{_}1369{_}In a state of Nature the dog does not even bark; he has acquired the art or knowledge from his companionship with man.
10020-8{_}1370{_}Isaiah compares the blind watchman of Israel to dogs, saying, "They are dumb; they cannot bark."
10020-8{_}1371{_}Again, to quote the argument of Dr. Gardiner: "The dog indicates his different feelings by different tones."
10020-8{_}1372{_}The following is his yelp when his foot is trod upon.
10020-8{_}1373{_}[Illustration] [Illustration: DOG YELPING.]
10020-8{_}1374{_}Haydn introduces the bark of a dog into the scherzo in his 38th quartette.
10020-8{_}1375{_}Indeed, the tones of the "voice" of the dog are so marked, that more than any other of the voices of Nature they have been utilized in music.
10020-8{_}1376{_}The merest tyro in the study of dog language can readily distinguish between the bark of joy--the "deep-mouthed welcome as we draw near home," as Byron put it--and the angry snarl, the yelp of pain, or the accents of fear.
10020-8{_}1377{_}Indeed, according to an assertion in the "Library of Entertaining Knowledge," the horse knows from the bark of a dog when he may expect an attack on his heels.
10020-8{_}1378{_}Gardiner suggests that it would be worth while to study the language of the dog.
10020-8{_}1379{_}Perhaps Professor Garnier, when he has reduced the language of the monkey to "A, B, C," might feel inclined to take up the matter.
10020-8{_}1380{_}[Illustration] [Illustration: THE OX.]
10020-8{_}1381{_}Next to the dog there is no animal in which there is more variation of sound than in oxen: "Their lowing, though rough and rude, is music to the farmer's ear save one who moans the loss of her sportive young; with wandering eye and anxious look she grieves the livelong day."
10020-8{_}1382{_}It is specially difficult in the case of oxen to suppose that they have a language; but it is impossible to doubt that the variations of their lowing are understood of one another, and serve to express their feelings if not their thoughts.
10020-8{_}1383{_}[Illustration] [Illustration: COW LOWING.]
10020-8{_}1384{_}In the matter of exclamations, one knows how readily these may be imitated upon the violin, or in the case of the deeper or more guttural sounds, on the violoncello.
10020-8{_}1385{_}The natural effect is greatly aided by the sliding of the finger along the note, especially in the case of the lowing of cattle; but there are other exclamations that are readily reduced to music.
10020-8{_}1386{_}Gardiner gives one or two interesting cases, and the common salutation, "How d'ye do?"
10020-8{_}1387{_}may be instanced.
10020-8{_}1388{_}It usually starts on B natural, and the voice rising to D ends on C; whereas, the reply, "Pretty well, thank you," begins on D, and falling to A, ends again on D. After a few attempts on the piano, the reader will be able readily to form these notes for himself.
10020-8{_}1389{_}[Illustration] [Illustration: HORSE NEIGHING.]
10020-8{_}1390{_}The horse, on the other hand, is rarely heard, and, though having a piercing whinny which passes through every semitone of the scale, it is scarcely ever varied.
10020-8{_}1391{_}[Illustration: THE CHIRP OF THE GRASSHOPPER.]
10020-8{_}1392{_}The music of the insects has already been alluded to, and everyone will agree with Gilbert White that "not undelightful is the ceaseless hum, to him who musing walks at noon."
10020-8{_}1393{_}The entomologist has laboured hard to show us that the insect has no voice, and that the "drowsy hum" is made by the wings; a fact which, being beyond all cavil, puts to the blush the old-world story of Plutarch, who tells us that when Terpander was playing upon the lyre, at the Olympic games, and had enraptured his audience to the highest pitch of enthusiasm a string of his instrument broke, and a _cicada_ or grasshopper perched on the bridge supplied by its voice the loss of the string and saved the fame of the musician.
10020-8{_}1394{_}To this day in Surinam the Dutch call them lyre-players.
10020-8{_}1395{_}If there is any truth in the story, the grasshopper then had powers far in advance of his degenerated descendants; for now the grasshopper--like the cricket--has a chirp consisting of three notes in rhythm, always forming a triplet in the key of B.
10020-8{_}1396{_}[Illustration] [Illustration: FLY BUZZING.]
10020-8{_}1397{_}[Illustration] [Illustration: DUCK.]
10020-8{_}1398{_}Gardiner, on the authority of Dr. Primatt, states that, to produce the sound it makes, the house-fly must make 320 vibrations of its wings in a second; or nearly 20,000 if it continues on the wing a minute.
10020-8{_}1399{_}The sound is invariably on the note F in the first space.
10020-8{_}1400{_}The music of a duck's note is given in the annexed score.
10020-8{_}1401{_}In conclusion, an article on the music of Nature would not be complete without an allusion to the music of the winds and the storm.
10020-8{_}1402{_}Admirers of Beethoven will recall numerous passages that would serve as illustrations.
10020-8{_}1403{_}One particularly might be mentioned--the chorus in "Judah" (Haydn), "The Lord devoureth them all," which is admirably imitative of the reverberations of the cataract and the thundering of mighty waters.
10020-8{_}1404{_}The sounds at sea, ominous of shipwreck, will also occur to the minds of some.
10020-8{_}1405{_}At Land's End it is not uncommon for storms to be heralded by weird sounds; and in the northern seas sailors, always a superstitious race of people, used to be much alarmed by a singular musical effect, which is now well known to be caused by nothing more fearsome than a whale breathing.
10020-8{_}1406{_}These instances might be still further multiplied, but enough have, perhaps, been given to excite some general interest in "the _Music of Nature_."
10020-8{_}1407{_}*       *       *       *       *    _Portraits of Celebrities at Different Times of Their Lives._   SIR HENRY LOCH.
10020-8{_}1408{_}BORN 1827.
10020-8{_}1409{_}Sir Henry Brougham Loch, G.C.M.G., K.C.B., whose name has recently been so prominently before the public in connection with the disturbances in Mashonaland, is Chief Commissioner at the Cape.
10020-8{_}1410{_}In his diplomatic career he was taken prisoner during the war with China; and, with Mr. Boulby, the _Times_ correspondent, was carried about in a cage by his captors, and exhibited to the natives.
10020-8{_}1411{_}After his liberation he returned to England, and was appointed Governor of the Isle of Man, and subsequently Governor of Victoria; and, in 1889, was appointed to succeed Sir Hercules Robinson as Chief Commissioner at the Cape.
10020-8{_}1412{_}[Illustration: AGE 22.
10020-8{_}1413{_}_From a Painting._]  [Illustration: AGE 39.
10020-8{_}1414{_}_From a Painting by G. Richmond, R.A._]  [Illustration: PRESENT DAY.
10020-8{_}1415{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}1416{_}by Foster & Martin, Melbourne._]   MADAME BELLE COLE.
10020-8{_}1417{_}It was in Jubilee Year that the British public were first charmed by the singing of this admirable American contralto.
10020-8{_}1418{_}She sang in London, and successive audiences were quick to confirm the judgments of Sir Joseph Barnby and certain other critics who had heard her only in private.
10020-8{_}1419{_}Her advance to the front rank of English singers was exceedingly rapid, and her position amongst us was long since made secure.
10020-8{_}1420{_}Madame Cole has taken part in nearly all the great musical events in this country during the past four years.
10020-8{_}1421{_}She has sung everywhere in London--with the Royal Choral Society at the Albert Hall, at the Handel Festival at the Crystal Palace, at the Ballad Concerts, at the Monday Popular Concerts, at Sir Charles Hall's Concerts, and at Bristol, Chester, Leeds, Birmingham, and other leading towns.
10020-8{_}1422{_}As seems to have been the case with most well-dowered musicians, Madame Cole's talent owes something to heredity.
10020-8{_}1423{_}Musical ability, greater or less, may at all events be traced back in her family for a considerable period.
10020-8{_}1424{_}Madame Cole's first distinct success in public was gained with Mr. Theodore Thomas, during that gentleman's first "grand transcontinental tour from ocean to ocean" in 1883.

10020-8{_}1430{_}by Walery, Regent Street._]   THE LORD BISHOP OF PETERBOROUGH.
10020-8{_}1431{_}BORN 1843.
10020-8{_}1432{_}Professor the Rev.
10020-8{_}1433{_}Mandell Creighton, M.A., was born at Carlisle, and educated at Durham Grammar School and Merton College, Oxford.
10020-8{_}1434{_}He was ordained deacon in 1870 and priest in 1873, and in 1875 accepted the living of Embleton, in Northumberland.
10020-8{_}1435{_}In 1884 he was elected to the newly founded professorship of Ecclesiastical History in the University of Cambridge.
10020-8{_}1436{_}In 1885 he was appointed by the Crown canon residentiary of Worcester Cathedral.
10020-8{_}1437{_}He is the author of several historical works: "Primer of Roman History," 1875; "The Age of Elizabeth," 1876; etc.
10020-8{_}1438{_}His principal work is a "History of the Papacy During the Period of the Reformation."
10020-8{_}1439{_}He was appointed Bishop of Peterborough in 1891.

10020-8{_}1448{_}by Elliott & Fry._]   LORD WANTAGE.
10020-8{_}1449{_}BORN 1832.
10020-8{_}1450{_}Robert James Loyd-Lindsay, K.C.B., V.C.
10020-8{_}1451{_}is the eldest son of the late Lieut.-General James Lindsay.
10020-8{_}1452{_}He was educated at Eton, and at an early age entered the Army.
10020-8{_}1453{_}He served in the Guinea, 1854-5, part of the time as _Aide-de-Camp_ to the Commander-in-Chief.
10020-8{_}1454{_}At the battle of Alma, amidst great disorder, he reformed the line and stood firm with the colours.
10020-8{_}1455{_}At Inkerman he distinguished himself by charging and repulsing a strong body of Russians with a few men; for which distinctions he was justly awarded the Victoria Cross.
10020-8{_}1456{_}Lord Wantage was Equerry to the Prince of Wales, 1858-9; and has been Extra Equerry to His Royal Highness since 1874.
10020-8{_}1457{_}He is also the Lord Lieutenant and a County Councillor of Berkshire.
10020-8{_}1458{_}He married, in 1858, Harriet Sarah, only child of the first Baron Overstone.

10020-8{_}1464{_}_From a Photograph by W. & A. H. Fry, Brighton._]   SIR RICHARD TEMPLE, BART, M.P.
10020-8{_}1465{_}BORN 1826.
10020-8{_}1466{_}Sir Richard Temple, Bart., G.C.S.I., M.P., D.C.L.
10020-8{_}1467{_}(Oxon), LL.D.
10020-8{_}1468{_}(Cantab), of The Nash, Kempsey, near Worcester, entered the third class of the Bengal Civil Service in 1846.
10020-8{_}1469{_}He was Secretary to Sir John Lawrence in the Punjab, and eventually was appointed Chief Commissioner of the Central Provinces, and the Political Resident at Hyderabad.
10020-8{_}1470{_}He was Foreign Secretary to the Governor-General, and Finance Minister of India, from 1868 to 1874.
10020-8{_}1471{_}In January, 1874, he was appointed to superintend the relief operations in the famine-stricken districts of Bengal.
10020-8{_}1472{_}He became Lieutenant-Governor of Bengal in 1875; was created a Baronet in August, 1876; and was appointed Governor of the Presidency of Bombay in January, 1877, which office he held till March, 1880.
10020-8{_}1473{_}He sits for the Kingston Division of Surrey.

10020-8{_}1481{_}by Elliott & Fry._]         *       *       *       *       *    _A Terrible New Year's Eve._  BY KATHLEEN HUDDLESTON.
10020-8{_}1482{_}In a little Belgian village not many miles from Brussels the winter sun shone brightly.
10020-8{_}1483{_}It shone through the quaint old windows of a little, red-tiled cottage, and on the figure of a girl who stood in the centre of the kitchen reading a long, closely written letter.
10020-8{_}1484{_}Over the blazing fire, where the "pot au feu" was simmering, bent an old woman, and the girl's voice came joyously to her as she stirred the savoury mess.
10020-8{_}1485{_}[Illustration: "MY AUNT, PAUL HAS SENT FOR ME."]
10020-8{_}1486{_}"My aunt, Paul has sent for me.
10020-8{_}1487{_}At last he has got permanent work.
10020-8{_}1488{_}It is nothing very great at present, but it may lead to better things, and the pay is enough, with what he has saved, to enable him to rent a little 'appartement.'
10020-8{_}1489{_}If I can, he wants me, with our little Pierre, to catch the coach at 'Les Trois Frres' to-morrow.
10020-8{_}1490{_}We should then reach Brussels by night and spend our New Year together."
10020-8{_}1491{_}As Babette spoke, her cheeks all flushed with hope and joy, the eyes of both the women rested on a cradle that stood in the room.
10020-8{_}1492{_}In this, baby Pierre, only a twelvemonth old, lay sleeping peacefully.
10020-8{_}1493{_}Then said the old woman, sadly, "I shall miss you, dearest, and the baby too.
10020-8{_}1494{_}Still, it is only right you should go.
10020-8{_}1495{_}Perhaps in the summer you may return for a bit.
10020-8{_}1496{_}Time passes quickly.
10020-8{_}1497{_}A year ago you were weeping over Paul's departure; and now, behold, you are going to join him, and lay in his arms the son he has never seen."
10020-8{_}1498{_}Babette nodded.
10020-8{_}1499{_}She was between tears and smiles.
10020-8{_}1500{_}There was grief, true and deep, at leaving the dear old aunt, who had been so good to her and to her child.
10020-8{_}1501{_}There was joy at the thought of seeing again the brave young husband whom she had wedded in the little village church two years before, and from whom the parting had been so bitter, when he left her, just before the birth of their baby boy, to seek work in the Belgian capital.
10020-8{_}1502{_}But there was no time to waste.
10020-8{_}1503{_}After the simple mid-day meal there were many things to be done, and all through the short winter day they were busy.
10020-8{_}1504{_}There was a bundle of warm wraps to be put together for Babette to take with her.
10020-8{_}1505{_}Her little trunk, with Pierre's cradle, and some odds and ends of furniture, would follow in a few days, when her aunt had collected and packed them all.
10020-8{_}1506{_}Her little store of money was counted over.
10020-8{_}1507{_}Alas!
10020-8{_}1508{_}it was very slender.
10020-8{_}1509{_}She must travel quickly and cheaply if it was to last her till she reached Brussels.
10020-8{_}1510{_}"Jean's cart will take you as far as 'Les Trois Frres,'" said the old lady, cheerfully, after finding that counting the little heap of francs and half-francs over and over did not increase them.
10020-8{_}1511{_}"That will save something.
10020-8{_}1512{_}You can catch the coach that stops there at two, and by six you will be in Brussels.
10020-8{_}1513{_}I pray the little one may not take cold."
10020-8{_}1514{_}Babette agreed to all her aunt suggested.
10020-8{_}1515{_}Jean was a farmer of the village; well-to-do and good-natured.
10020-8{_}1516{_}She knew he would gladly give her a seat in his waggon, which was going next day to "Les Trois Frres," an inn six miles from the village.
10020-8{_}1517{_}The coach for Brussels stopped there twice a week, and when once she had taken her place in it, the worst of her journey would be over.
10020-8{_}1518{_}They went to rest early that night, and by eleven next morning the last good-bye had been said.
10020-8{_}1519{_}Pretty Babette was seated by the side of Farmer Jean, with her baby boy, wrapped up in numerous shawls, clasped tightly to her, and the great Flemish horses were plodding, slowly but surely, towards "Les Trois Frres".
10020-8{_}1520{_}The day was not as bright as the preceding one.
10020-8{_}1521{_}Snow had fallen during the night, and the sky looked heavy, as though there were more to come.
10020-8{_}1522{_}Babette shivered, in spite of her long, warm cloak.
10020-8{_}1523{_}The roads were freezing hard, but they managed to proceed for a mile or two, and then suddenly there came a sway and a lurch, for one of the horses had slipped and fallen on the snowy road, and the other was trying to free himself from his struggling companion by frantic kicks and plunges.
10020-8{_}1524{_}Farmer Jean had a man with him, and between them they got the poor animal up, while Babette stood in the cold highway, her baby peeping wonderingly from the folds of her cloak.
10020-8{_}1525{_}The horse was bruised and cut about the knees, but otherwise unhurt, so the men resumed their places; Babette climbed back to hers, and the heavy cart went jolting on.
10020-8{_}1526{_}The farmer cracked his whip, and whenever the road grew worse he or his man got down and led the horses.
10020-8{_}1527{_}In spite of this, their progress grew slower and slower.
10020-8{_}1528{_}"I don't like to say so," said the master, "but we've two more miles to go, and it is past one o'clock now.
10020-8{_}1529{_}My girl, if the coach is gone, I'll get you back and drive you in again next time it passes."
10020-8{_}1530{_}But Babette would not hear of this.
10020-8{_}1531{_}Not to see Paul by nightfall!
10020-8{_}1532{_}Not to be clasped in his arms, she and little Pierre together, in one warm embrace!
10020-8{_}1533{_}Not to spend New Year's Day with him!
10020-8{_}1534{_}No!
10020-8{_}1535{_}she would not think of it.
10020-8{_}1536{_}And yet when, more than an hour later, they rolled into the yard of "Les Trois Frres," there was no sign of the Brussels coach.
10020-8{_}1537{_}It had started half an hour before.
10020-8{_}1538{_}"Les Trois Frres" was a quiet, homely inn, little used excepting when the coach stopped there.
10020-8{_}1539{_}Babette, pale and trembling, got down and ran into the bar, where the landlord stood smiling behind a row of bright pewter taps.
10020-8{_}1540{_}"Am I too late for the coach?"
10020-8{_}1541{_}she cried.
10020-8{_}1542{_}"Has it gone?"
10020-8{_}1543{_}And then, when the man told her she was indeed too late, all strength and energy left her, and she sank sobbing on the wooden bench by the door.
10020-8{_}1544{_}There were two other men in the room, who looked at her curiously; she was such a pretty girl, even in the midst of her grief.
10020-8{_}1545{_}One was an old pedlar, with his well-filled pack on the floor beside him.
10020-8{_}1546{_}He had a pleasant, homely face, and thin, bent figure.
10020-8{_}1547{_}The other was a middle-sized, powerful fellow, clean shaven and beetle-browed, and dressed in shabby, ill-fitting garments.
10020-8{_}1548{_}It was hard to tell what his rank in life might be.
10020-8{_}1549{_}He stared once again at Babette, and then handed his glass to the host to be re-filled.
10020-8{_}1550{_}The pedlar was the first to break the silence.
10020-8{_}1551{_}[Illustration: "'CHEER UP, MY LASS', HE SAID KINDLY."]
10020-8{_}1552{_}"Cheer up, my lass," he said, kindly; "I too have missed the coach, and I too must reach Brussels to-night.
10020-8{_}1553{_}I have two thousand francs in notes and gold in my pocketbook, which are the savings of a lifetime, and I am going to pay them into the bank tomorrow.
10020-8{_}1554{_}Then I shall give up my trade and start a little shop."
10020-8{_}1555{_}"I would not talk too much about them in the meantime, friend.
10020-8{_}1556{_}In some countries it might be dangerous, but we are honest in Belgium."
10020-8{_}1557{_}It was the other man who spoke, and his voice, though rough, was not unpleasant.
10020-8{_}1558{_}He paid the landlord, caught up his stick, and with a curt "Good-day" passed out of "Les Trois Frres."
10020-8{_}1559{_}"He, also, perhaps, is going to Brussels.
10020-8{_}1560{_}He means to walk, and if he, why not I?"
10020-8{_}1561{_}said the pedlar.
10020-8{_}1562{_}He had come in cold and tired, and the landlord's good ale had made him slightly loquacious.
10020-8{_}1563{_}"Yes, I shall try and walk.
10020-8{_}1564{_}The roads are better walking than driving.
10020-8{_}1565{_}It is not so very many miles, and most likely I shall be overtaken by some cart going the same way."
10020-8{_}1566{_}And he rose as he spoke.
10020-8{_}1567{_}Babette rose also and caught him eagerly by the hand.
10020-8{_}1568{_}"I will walk with you," she cried.
10020-8{_}1569{_}"I am strong, well shod, and the fastest walker in our village.
10020-8{_}1570{_}We can get to Brussels before dark, in spite of my having my boy to carry.
10020-8{_}1571{_}Oh!
10020-8{_}1572{_}bless you for thinking of it, for now I shall see Paul before the year is out."
10020-8{_}1573{_}Nor would she be dissuaded.
10020-8{_}1574{_}Farmer Jean came in and said something about snow.
10020-8{_}1575{_}"The sky was darkening for it already."
10020-8{_}1576{_}But Babette was firm.
10020-8{_}1577{_}The landlord's buxom wife came forth from an inner room and offered her a lodging for the night, and then, when she could not persuade her, helped her to wrap the baby up afresh, and finally made her place in her pocket a tiny flask of brandy, "in case," she said, "the snow should overtake them."
10020-8{_}1578{_}So they started.
10020-8{_}1579{_}Babette had spoken the truth when she called herself a good walker.
10020-8{_}1580{_}She was but twenty, and was both slight and active.
10020-8{_}1581{_}The pedlar too, in spite of his bent form, got over the ground quickly.
10020-8{_}1582{_}They had put four or five good miles between themselves and "Les Trois Frres" when the snow began to fall.
10020-8{_}1583{_}It came down steadily in thick, heavy flakes.
10020-8{_}1584{_}Babette drew her cloak yet closer round her boy and they plodded on, but walking became more and more difficult, and they grew both weary and cold.
10020-8{_}1585{_}Suddenly, by the roadside, several yards ahead, they saw a man's figure.
10020-8{_}1586{_}He was coming to meet them, and drew near rapidly, and then they recognised their friend in the shabby brown clothes, who had left the inn so shortly before them.
10020-8{_}1587{_}"I saw you coming," he explained, "so came to meet you.
10020-8{_}1588{_}Madame"--with a bow to Babette, polite for one so uncouth looking--"can go no further to-night; the storm will not pass off yet.
10020-8{_}1589{_}I live not far from here with my mother and brothers, and if madame likes, we can all take shelter under my humble roof.
10020-8{_}1590{_}It is but a poor place, but you will be welcome, and doubtless we can find two spare beds."
10020-8{_}1591{_}They could do nothing but thank him and accept his offer.
10020-8{_}1592{_}Even Babette acknowledged that all hope of reaching Brussels was now over.
10020-8{_}1593{_}The New Year would have dawned before she and her husband met.
10020-8{_}1594{_}The wind had risen and the snow, half turned to sleet, was now beating furiously into their faces.
10020-8{_}1595{_}It was all they could do to keep their feet.
10020-8{_}1596{_}They struggled on after their guide as best they could, till he turned out of the high road into a lane; and thankful were they when he stopped, and, pushing open a gate that creaked on rusty hinges, led them up a narrow, gravelled pathway to a small, bare house, flanked on either side by some dreary bushes of evergreens.
10020-8{_}1597{_}In answer to his peremptory knock, the door was opened by a man slighter and shorter than himself, but sufficiently like him to be known as his brother, and the travellers staggered in--the door, with a heavy crash, blowing to behind them.
10020-8{_}1598{_}Perhaps now for the first time it really struck Babette that she had been headstrong in persisting in her journey, and in trusting herself and child to the mercy of utter strangers so far from home.
10020-8{_}1599{_}The same thought passed through the old pedlar's mind, but it was too late to retreat, so they silently followed their new host and his brother.
10020-8{_}1600{_}They went down a passage and into a room, half kitchen, half parlour, snugly and even comfortably furnished.
10020-8{_}1601{_}[Illustration: "A MAN AND A WOMAN SAT OVER THE FIRE."]
10020-8{_}1602{_}Heavy wooden shutters dulled the noise of the boisterous gale outside.
10020-8{_}1603{_}A thick red curtain hung over the door, and a cheery log fire burnt in the stove.
10020-8{_}1604{_}A man and woman sat over it; the man, a tall, repulsive-looking creature, with unkempt hair and matted beard, his age apparently about fifty.
10020-8{_}1605{_}The woman looked seventy or more.
10020-8{_}1606{_}She too had once been tall, but now old age gave her a withered, witch-like appearance, in spite of her great height.
10020-8{_}1607{_}She was dressed in limp, faded garments, with a tattered shawl crossed over her chest, and had a scared, miserable look in her bleared old eyes.
10020-8{_}1608{_}There were a few words of explanation from the man who had come home, and then, in gruff but not unkindly tones, he bade Babette be seated, and told his mother to get some supper speedily.
10020-8{_}1609{_}She spread a coarse cloth on the wooden table, and when all was ready, lifted a large black saucepan from the stove and turned out a smoking, savoury-looking stew.
10020-8{_}1610{_}The youngest son produced a bottle containing the thin acid wine of the country, and another of spirits.
10020-8{_}1611{_}As he set them on the table, Babette noticed that across one of his hands, which were much smaller and whiter than those of his brothers, there ran a dull red scar that looked as if he had had a bad cut there.
10020-8{_}1612{_}Then they all sat down, excepting the old mother, who busied herself in waiting on them.
10020-8{_}1613{_}"It's the last good meal you'll get for some time, I'm thinking," she croaked, as she watched them devouring their supper, "unless you turn to and find more work than you've done lately.
10020-8{_}1614{_}The landlord called for his rent again to-day and swore he would wait no longer, but turn us out if we did not pay in three days' time."
10020-8{_}1615{_}"Curse him!"
10020-8{_}1616{_}muttered the man who had brought the strangers in, half under his breath; then aloud he added, "Shut up, good mother: remember, we have visitors; and one a man of property, who will hardly sympathize with our poverty."
10020-8{_}1617{_}Babette looked up as he spoke, and intercepted a glance so strange and savage that passed between the brothers and then rested on her friend the pedlar, that involuntarily she shuddered and turned pale.
10020-8{_}1618{_}The old man, however, did not appear to notice anything unsatisfactory in the appearance or manners of his hosts.
10020-8{_}1619{_}He had eaten to his liking, and had allowed the grim-looking eldest brother to fill his glass again and again with "Genievre" till his face began to flush, and his eyes grew dazed and heavy.
10020-8{_}1620{_}Babette felt more and more uneasy.
10020-8{_}1621{_}Oh!
10020-8{_}1622{_}to be back at "Les Trois Frres" again, or even out in the snowy road!
10020-8{_}1623{_}Anything would be better than sitting in this lonely house, with those three forbidding faces glaring on her.
10020-8{_}1624{_}She rose hastily and caught up her sleeping child.
10020-8{_}1625{_}"I am very tired, good people," she said, timidly, "and I must start betimes in the morning.
10020-8{_}1626{_}If I might go to bed now, I should be so thankful."
10020-8{_}1627{_}In answer to her request, the old woman lighted a candle, and Babette followed her upstairs into a small, low chamber.
10020-8{_}1628{_}There was no superfluous furniture in it, but the little bed looked clean and inviting, and the curtains that hung in front of the tiny window were made of light, fresh-looking chintz.
10020-8{_}1629{_}Facing the bed was a door, leading apparently into another room.
10020-8{_}1630{_}Babette wondered if it was the one her friend the pedlar was to occupy, but she was not long left in doubt.
10020-8{_}1631{_}The old woman wished her good-night and left her, and Babette, after hushing her boy to sleep again, had just sunk wearily into the one chair the room boasted, when she heard a slow, heavy step ascending, and knew the pedlar was coming to bed.
10020-8{_}1632{_}He shut the outer door behind him, and began arranging his pack.
10020-8{_}1633{_}Babette could hear the pedlar moving backwards and forwards with uncertain, tired footsteps.
10020-8{_}1634{_}There was no sound below, even the wind was hushed.
10020-8{_}1635{_}She drew aside the curtains and looked out, and saw that the snow had ceased to fall, and lay thick and white on the ground.
10020-8{_}1636{_}Then there came a sudden presentiment upon her.
10020-8{_}1637{_}A sense of danger, vague and undefined, seemed to surround her.
10020-8{_}1638{_}It was all the more terrible on account of its vagueness.
10020-8{_}1639{_}She did not know what she feared, yet the terror of something horrible was strong upon her.
10020-8{_}1640{_}She slipped off her boots, and stole gently up to the door that divided her room from the pedlar's.
10020-8{_}1641{_}"Sir," she whispered, "you are very, very tired, and will sleep heavily.
10020-8{_}1642{_}I am so anxious, I don't know why; but forgive me and do trust me.
10020-8{_}1643{_}Push your pocket-book that contains your money under the door.
10020-8{_}1644{_}See--it does not fit tight!
10020-8{_}1645{_}We don't know who the people of the house are: they may try to rob you.
10020-8{_}1646{_}I will tie it up inside my baby's shawls, and will give it back to you as soon as we are out of this place.
10020-8{_}1647{_}Oh, would to God that we had never entered it!
10020-8{_}1648{_}Your money will be safe with me, and they will never think of looking for it here.
10020-8{_}1649{_}Will you give it me?"
10020-8{_}1650{_}In answer to her pleadings, a shabby little leather book was pushed into her room.
10020-8{_}1651{_}As she picked it up and proceeded to hide it securely away beneath the baby's many wrappings, the pedlar said, in a voice rendered hoarse and indistinct by the spirits he had partaken of in such unaccustomed quantities: "Here, my dear, take it.
10020-8{_}1652{_}It will, I know, be safe with you.
10020-8{_}1653{_}I feel so tired that I don't think a cannon would wake me to-night once I get to sleep."
10020-8{_}1654{_}He groped his way to his bed, and flung himself down on it, dressed as he was.
10020-8{_}1655{_}Soon Babette heard him snoring loudly and regularly, and then she took off her clothes, and rolling her cloak around her, lay down by the side of her child.
10020-8{_}1656{_}In after years, when she looked on that awful time, she often wondered how, feeling as she did that she was surrounded by so many unknown perils, she had ever closed her eyes.
10020-8{_}1657{_}Perhaps the long walk and the excitement she had undergone accounted for the profound sleep into which she fell almost immediately, and from which she was aroused in the dead of night by a noise in the next room.
10020-8{_}1658{_}It was neither snore nor cry.
10020-8{_}1659{_}It was more like a long, shuddering gurgle, and then--silence!
10020-8{_}1660{_}Frightful, terrible silence, broken at last by the sound of stealthy footsteps and hushed voices.
10020-8{_}1661{_}Babette sunk down on her pillow again, her baby clutched in her arms.
10020-8{_}1662{_}A voiceless prayer went up to Heaven for the child's safety and her own, for already she heard them approaching her door, and made sure her last hour was come.
10020-8{_}1663{_}Through nearly closed eyelids she watched two of the men enter; the one who had brought them to the house and his elder brother.
10020-8{_}1664{_}They were muttering curses, low but deep.
10020-8{_}1665{_}"To have risked so much for nothing!"
10020-8{_}1666{_}whispered one.
10020-8{_}1667{_}"Can she have it, or was the old fool jesting with us?"
10020-8{_}1668{_}"It's a jest that has cost him dear," answered the other, as he watched his brother search the girl's clothes and then slip his murderous hands beneath her pillow.
10020-8{_}1669{_}He withdrew them empty.
10020-8{_}1670{_}"Shall we settle her?"
10020-8{_}1671{_}he asked, "or let her go?
10020-8{_}1672{_}Is it not best to be on the safe side?"
10020-8{_}1673{_}But the smooth-shaven one said, decisively: "Let her alone; we have enough to answer for.
10020-8{_}1674{_}See, she is sound asleep, and if not, it will be easy to find out before she reaches Brussels how much she knows.
10020-8{_}1675{_}Let her be."
10020-8{_}1676{_}Babette lay like a log, stirring neither hand nor foot.
10020-8{_}1677{_}In that awful moment, when her life or death was trembling in the balance, her mother love, that divine instinct implanted in every woman's breast, came to her and saved her.
10020-8{_}1678{_}She knew that if she moved her baby's life was gone--her own she hardly cared about just then.
10020-8{_}1679{_}But those little limbs that were nestling so soft and warm against her own, and that little flaxen head that was cuddled against her arm, for their sake she was brave.
10020-8{_}1680{_}[Illustration: "SHE LAY MOTIONLESS"]  So she lay motionless and listened, fearing that the men would hear even the quick, heavy throbs of her heart.
10020-8{_}1681{_}But they did not.
10020-8{_}1682{_}They searched quickly and systematically amongst all her clothing.
10020-8{_}1683{_}They felt under her pillow again, but never thought of looking at the shawls of the baby who lay so peacefully by her side; and then at last they crept away and closed the door gently behind them.
10020-8{_}1684{_}The room was in utter darkness.
10020-8{_}1685{_}For ages, as it seemed, Babette lay there, afraid to stir, and listening vainly for some sound; then she sat up, all white and trembling.
10020-8{_}1686{_}"My God!"
10020-8{_}1687{_}she thought.
10020-8{_}1688{_}"What awful thing has happened?
10020-8{_}1689{_}Oh, give me strength and courage, for my baby's sake."
10020-8{_}1690{_}As an inspiration, there came to her the thought of the little bottle that the good-natured landlady of "Les Trois Frres" had given her.
10020-8{_}1691{_}She felt in the pocket of her dress and drew it out, taking a long, deep draught of the fiery spirit.
10020-8{_}1692{_}She had been on the verge of fainting, though she knew it not, and the brandy put new life into her.
10020-8{_}1693{_}She listened for a long time and then gently--very gently--she crept out of bed and drew aside the little curtain from the window.
10020-8{_}1694{_}Perhaps a wild idea of escaping into the cold, dark night outside, aided by a sheet or blanket, flashed through her brain.
10020-8{_}1695{_}If so, she soon realized that it would not be practicable.
10020-8{_}1696{_}The window was not high, but it was small, and divided by thick, old-fashioned bars of iron.
10020-8{_}1697{_}To get out was impossible.
10020-8{_}1698{_}[Illustration: "SHE STOOD CONSIDERING."]
10020-8{_}1699{_}As she stood considering, a thin, flickering moonbeam crept in and partially lighted up the room.
10020-8{_}1700{_}It fell on to the door that led into the pedlar's chamber, and showed her something dark and slimy that was flowing slowly--slowly from under it into her room.
10020-8{_}1701{_}She did not cry out or fall senseless.
10020-8{_}1702{_}She bent down and put her hand into it, and saw that it was blood--her poor old friend's life-blood--for she knew now beyond all doubt that he had been murdered for the sake of his supposed wealth.
10020-8{_}1703{_}She knew she was helpless till morning.
10020-8{_}1704{_}To get out of the house was impossible, for to do so she must pass down the stairs and through the room below, where probably they were either sleeping or watching.
10020-8{_}1705{_}If she had courage and could only let them think she knew and suspected nothing, she might still escape.
10020-8{_}1706{_}Surely they would not dare to murder her also, for they knew her husband would be expecting her next day, and would be looking for her if she did not come.
10020-8{_}1707{_}With another prayer, this time uttered shiveringly, for the soul of the pedlar, she nerved herself to get into bed again, and lay there till morning with her child against her heart; gazing with staring, sleepless eyes at the door which divided her from that awful room; keeping surely the most terrible vigil that ever woman kept.
10020-8{_}1708{_}At last the morning dawned, clear and bright.
10020-8{_}1709{_}A frost had set in, and the roads were clean and hard, the sky was blue.
10020-8{_}1710{_}If it had not been for that ghastly stain that had crept across the far end of her room, she might almost have thought that the events of the night had been but a fearful dream.
10020-8{_}1711{_}Her child awoke, fresh and smiling, and she could hear them stirring in the living room below.
10020-8{_}1712{_}She felt that now, indeed, the hardest part of her task was still before her.
10020-8{_}1713{_}On a little table by the side of her bed there was a small, cracked looking-glass.
