10020-8{_}1714{_}When she was dressed she looked into it and saw that it reflected a face death-like in its pallor, with burning lips and feverish eyes.
10020-8{_}1715{_}She took the bottle from her pocket again and gulped down the rest of its contents.
10020-8{_}1716{_}It sent a flush into her cheeks and steadied the sick trembling that was shaking her through and through.
10020-8{_}1717{_}Without stopping to think or look round again, she took up her boy and descended the stairs, and entered the room where they had supped on the previous night.
10020-8{_}1718{_}The old woman was its sole occupant now.
10020-8{_}1719{_}She was bending over the fire frying something for breakfast, and the table in the centre of the room was prepared for the meal.
10020-8{_}1720{_}She looked if possible more untidy and slovenly than when Babette had last seen her, and greeted the girl with a feeble smile.
10020-8{_}1721{_}Then she poured her out a cup of coffee, and Babette had sat down and begun to sip it (for she knew she must make a pretence of breakfasting) when the eldest son came in.
10020-8{_}1722{_}There was a very uneasy look upon his evil-looking face.
10020-8{_}1723{_}"How are you?"
10020-8{_}1724{_}he asked, sullenly, as he sat down opposite her.
10020-8{_}1725{_}"I hope, rested.
10020-8{_}1726{_}Did you sleep well?"
10020-8{_}1727{_}Never afterwards did she know how she found courage to answer him as she did, quietly and firmly:--  "Yes, very well, thank you.
10020-8{_}1728{_}But my friend--he must have over-slept himself--why is he not down?"
10020-8{_}1729{_}The old woman dropped a plate with a clatter and turned round.
10020-8{_}1730{_}The man looked Babette straight in the face as he replied, and she met his glance with one just as steady.
10020-8{_}1731{_}"The pedlar is gone," he said, as he sugared his coffee carefully.
10020-8{_}1732{_}"He paid his bill and was off before seven.
10020-8{_}1733{_}You will probably see him in Brussels, for he was going there."
10020-8{_}1734{_}"Yes," repeated Babette, "I shall very likely meet him in Brussels, but I don't even know his name.
10020-8{_}1735{_}And I, too, good people, ought to be starting.
10020-8{_}1736{_}The morning is fine, and walking will be easy."
10020-8{_}1737{_}She drank down her coffee as she spoke and rose.
10020-8{_}1738{_}"I cannot eat," she exclaimed, seeing that they both looked suspiciously at the thick slice of currant-bread, that lay untouched on her plate.
10020-8{_}1739{_}"I think I am excited at the thought of seeing my husband again.
10020-8{_}1740{_}It seems so long since we parted, and now we shall meet so soon."
10020-8{_}1741{_}In her own ears her voice sounded far away and unnatural, but they did not seem to notice anything strange in her.
10020-8{_}1742{_}The old woman, with a meek "Thank you," took the humble payment she tendered, and they let her go; only the big, burly eldest son stood at the door and watched her as she went slowly down the little pathway and out through the creaking gate into the snowy road.
10020-8{_}1743{_}She only looked back once, and then she saw that a dingy signboard hung in front of the house.
10020-8{_}1744{_}The picture of what was meant for a cow, and had once been white, was depicted on it, and the words "A la Vache Blanche" were clumsily painted underneath.
10020-8{_}1745{_}So the house was an inn, evidently, and as Babette read the words she dimly remembered having heard, long ago, that there was an inn of that name not far from Brussels.
10020-8{_}1746{_}It was kept by some people named Marac, whose characters were anything but good, and who had been implicated in several robberies that had taken place some years before, although the utmost efforts of the police had failed to trace any crime directly home to them.
10020-8{_}1747{_}"Oh, heavens!
10020-8{_}1748{_}Why did I not see that sign last night?"
10020-8{_}1749{_}the girl thought, despairingly, as she trudged along the hard, frosty road.
10020-8{_}1750{_}"It would have saved his life and perhaps my reason."
10020-8{_}1751{_}She sped along faster and faster, for the house was now quite out of sight.
10020-8{_}1752{_}In the distance the way began to wind up-hill, and a stunted, leafless wood straggled along one side of the highway.
10020-8{_}1753{_}Babette was just considering whether going through it would shorten her journey, when a woman, dressed in the ordinary peasant costume of the country, emerged from it and came towards her with quick, firm steps.
10020-8{_}1754{_}She was tall and rather masculine looking.
10020-8{_}1755{_}The black Flemish cloak she wore hung round her in straight, thick folds.
10020-8{_}1756{_}She carried a market basket on one arm; a neat white cloth concealing the eggs and butter that probably lay underneath.
10020-8{_}1757{_}"Good-day," she said, in thick, guttural tones, as she reached Babette.
10020-8{_}1758{_}"Are you on the way to Brussels?"
10020-8{_}1759{_}Babette made way for her to pass, somewhat shyly.
10020-8{_}1760{_}"Yes," she said, "and I am in haste; but the roads are heavy and I have my baby to carry."
10020-8{_}1761{_}As she answered, her eyes happened to fall on the stranger's right hand, which was ungloved and clasping the basket.
10020-8{_}1762{_}And as she looked her heart seemed suddenly to quiver and stand still, for across that strong right hand there ran a deep red scar, precisely similar to the one she had noticed on the previous night on the hand of the youngest brother at the "Vache Blanche."
10020-8{_}1763{_}It did not take long for the whole horrible truth to flash across her.
10020-8{_}1764{_}Doubtless they had felt insecure after their terrible deed, and the youngest Marac had been dispatched after her, disguised as a woman, with instructions to way-lay her by some shorter cut, in order to find out if she was really ignorant of the frightful way in which the pedlar had met his untimely end.
10020-8{_}1765{_}As these thoughts chased each other through her mind, she felt as if her great terror was slowly blanching her face, and her limbs began to tremble till she could hardly drag herself over the ground.
10020-8{_}1766{_}But her baby's warm little heart, beating so closely against her own, once more gave her strength.
10020-8{_}1767{_}She dropped her eyes so that she might no longer see that awful hand, and tottered on by the new-comer's side, striving to imagine that it was indeed only a harmless peasant woman who was walking by her and trying to remember that every step was bringing her nearer to Brussels and protection.
10020-8{_}1768{_}Her companion glanced at her curiously, and Babette shivered, for she fancied she saw suspicion in the look.
10020-8{_}1769{_}"You seem tired."
10020-8{_}1770{_}she, or rather he, said, always speaking in the same low, thick tones.
10020-8{_}1771{_}"Brussels is barely two miles off, and it is yet early, but perhaps you have not rested well.
10020-8{_}1772{_}Where did you sleep?"
10020-8{_}1773{_}Too well did the girl know why that question was asked her, and now that her first sickening horror was over, her brave spirit nerved itself once more.
10020-8{_}1774{_}"I was journeying with a friend yesterday," she replied, "when the snow-storm overtook us.
10020-8{_}1775{_}Luckily we met a man whose home lay in our road.
10020-8{_}1776{_}He was very good, and took us there and gave us supper and beds."
10020-8{_}1777{_}The stranger laughed.
10020-8{_}1778{_}"A good Samaritan, indeed!
10020-8{_}1779{_}And your friend?
10020-8{_}1780{_}Where is he now?
10020-8{_}1781{_}Did he find his hosts so hospitable that he was unable to tear himself away?"
10020-8{_}1782{_}"No," said Babette, gently, "he started early; before I came down he was far on his road.
10020-8{_}1783{_}They were very good to me, and gave me coffee before I left.
10020-8{_}1784{_}I am a poor woman, and could do but little to repay them.
10020-8{_}1785{_}The two francs I gave them were almost my last."
10020-8{_}1786{_}This speech, uttered in such a soft, even voice--for Babette had schooled herself well by now--seemed to satisfy her companion, and they walked on side by side in silence for what seemed to the poor girl the longest hour she had ever passed.
10020-8{_}1787{_}At last, in the far distance there rose the spires and roofs of Brussels.
10020-8{_}1788{_}The chiming of church bells came gaily towards them through the frosty air, and Babette knew that her terrible journey was well-nigh ended.
10020-8{_}1789{_}At the entrance of the town the stranger stopped.
10020-8{_}1790{_}[Illustration: "GOOD-BYE."]
10020-8{_}1791{_}"Good-bye," she said, curtly; "I am late for the market, and must sell my eggs quickly or shall not get my price."
10020-8{_}1792{_}[Illustration: "SHE SANK DOWN IN A HEAVY, DEATH-LIKE SWOON."]
10020-8{_}1793{_}She turned down a side street and disappeared, and Babette felt her strength and mind both failing her now that she was out of danger.
10020-8{_}1794{_}She staggered weakly into a big, dim church, by the door of which the parting happened to have taken place.
10020-8{_}1795{_}Here she sank down in a heavy, death-like swoon in front of one of the side altars, with her baby wailing fretfully at her breast.
10020-8{_}1796{_}When she came to herself again she was seated in the sacristy, and her hair and face were wet with the water they had flung over her.
10020-8{_}1797{_}By her side stood a black-robed, kindly-faced cur and two or three women, who were trying to force some wine down her throat.
10020-8{_}1798{_}By degrees her strength came back, and she raised herself and asked piteously for her child.
10020-8{_}1799{_}Then, when he was in her arms, she told her story.
10020-8{_}1800{_}Wonder, horror, and bewilderment all dawned in turns on her hearers' countenances, and it was not until she unpinned her baby's shawls and handed the shabby pocket-book to the priest that they were quite certain they had not to deal with some poor, wandering lunatic.
10020-8{_}1801{_}But when the money had been looked at and replaced, then, indeed, they saw the necessity for prompt action.
10020-8{_}1802{_}The cur caught up his hat, and, after whispering a few words to the women, hurried out of the sacristy.
10020-8{_}1803{_}"He is gone to the police," said one.
10020-8{_}1804{_}"Poor child"--laying her hand caressingly on the girl's damp hair--"what hast thou not passed through!
10020-8{_}1805{_}Mercifully the mass was not over, so we found thee at once.
10020-8{_}1806{_}Lie still and rest.
10020-8{_}1807{_}Give me but thy husband's name and address, and in one little half-hour he shall be by thy side."
10020-8{_}1808{_}And so he was, and then, when she had been examined by the chief of the police and sobbed out her story all over again, from the shelter of Paul's broad arms, she felt safe at last.
10020-8{_}1809{_}She went peacefully home with her husband, and after a good night's rest in the little rooms he had taken for her, she was able to listen calmly when told next day of the capture of the whole Marac family.
10020-8{_}1810{_}They had been taken red-handed in their guilt, for had not the pedlar's body been found in a disused cellar under their house?
10020-8{_}1811{_}He was brought to Brussels to be buried, but his name was never known, and his money was never claimed.
10020-8{_}1812{_}Probably, as he had told Babette, he had been a friendless old man, wandering alone from place to place.
10020-8{_}1813{_}The police were generous.
10020-8{_}1814{_}Half his money was given to the poor and the rest was handed to Babette, and helped to furnish her new home.
10020-8{_}1815{_}A simple stone cross now marks the unknown pedlar's grave: but flowers bloom there abundantly, and though nameless, he is not forgotten.
10020-8{_}1816{_}Many a prayer is uttered for him both by Babette and her children, for the memory of that terrible New Year's Eve will never fade from her mind.
10020-8{_}1817{_}*       *       *       *       *    _Personal Reminiscences of Sir Andrew Clark._  BY E. H. PITCAIRN.
10020-8{_}1818{_}[Illustration: SIR ANDREW CLARK.]
10020-8{_}1819{_}With a heartfelt pang, hundreds read in an evening paper on October 20th of the serious illness of Sir Andrew Clark, so truly spoken of by George Eliot as "the beloved physician."
10020-8{_}1820{_}Only the previous day he had presided at the Annual Harveian Oration as President of the College of Physicians.
10020-8{_}1821{_}He had more than one warning by severe attacks of illness, and by the recurrence of very painful symptoms, that he was over-taxing his strength, but they were unheeded.
10020-8{_}1822{_}A patient once told him he had a horror of having a fit.
10020-8{_}1823{_}"Put it away," said Sir Andrew; "I always do."
10020-8{_}1824{_}There was only one person to whose fatigue and exhaustion he was indifferent that was himself.
10020-8{_}1825{_}It is said that he always hoped to die in his carriage or consulting-room, and it was in the latter, while talking with a lady (the Hon.
10020-8{_}1826{_}Miss Boscawen) about some charity, that he was seized with the illness which ended so fatally.
10020-8{_}1827{_}In his case it is no morbid curiosity which makes thousands interested in every detail concerning him.
10020-8{_}1828{_}On one day as many as six hundred people, several of whom were quite poor patients, called to ask how he was, and daily inquiries from all parts, including the Royal Family were a proof how much he was respected.
10020-8{_}1829{_}Very peacefully, on Monday, November 6th, about five o'clock, he passed away, and on the following Saturday, after a service at Westminster Abbey, he was buried at Essendon, near Camfield, the property he had so lately bought and where he spent his last holiday.
10020-8{_}1830{_}The world has already been told how the English nation showed their respect for the President of the College of Physicians, and in him the profession he so dearly loved was honoured.
10020-8{_}1831{_}What was the reason of this demonstration of respect?
10020-8{_}1832{_}Because individuals seem to have felt a sense of irreparable _loss_.
10020-8{_}1833{_}Very many have the idea that there are few others with his gifts who would respond in the same way to their demand for sympathy and help; for Sir Andrew's interest in each patient was real.
10020-8{_}1834{_}There was an attractive force about him, difficult to describe, and which only those who knew him could understand, for he was nothing if not original.
10020-8{_}1835{_}It is impossible in this brief sketch to give an adequate portrait of a great personality and to tell the story of his life's work.
10020-8{_}1836{_}I shall but try to mention some of his distinctive qualities and characteristics, illustrated by a few facts.
10020-8{_}1837{_}Two or three real incidents sometimes give a better idea of a man's character than pages of generalities.
10020-8{_}1838{_}[Illustration: THE GRAVE IN ESSENDON CHURCHYARD.
10020-8{_}1839{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}1840{_}by Mavor & Meredith._]  Sir Andrew was born at Aberdeen in October, 1826.
10020-8{_}1841{_}His father died when he was seven years old, and his mother at his birth.
10020-8{_}1842{_}To the end of his life he regretted never having known a mother's love.
10020-8{_}1843{_}His childhood, spent with two uncles, does not seem to have been very happy, and he had no brother or sister.
10020-8{_}1844{_}He was educated at Aberdeen and Edinburgh, and at the former place took his degree.
10020-8{_}1845{_}As a young man he gained first medals in anatomy, physiology, chemistry, botany, materia medica, surgery, pathology, and practice of physic.
10020-8{_}1846{_}At twenty-two, in very delicate health, he entered the Royal Navy as assistant-surgeon, and was appointed to the hospital at Haslar.
10020-8{_}1847{_}His subsequent medical career is pretty generally known.
10020-8{_}1848{_}He obtained almost every possible honour, culminating in the Presidency of the College of Physicians for the lengthy term of six years.
10020-8{_}1849{_}Sir Andrew was devoted to the College.
10020-8{_}1850{_}He made an excellent President, and a dignified, courteous, and just chairman.
10020-8{_}1851{_}His successor will find it no easy task to fill his place.
10020-8{_}1852{_}He took an intense interest in all that concerned the welfare of the College, and gave many proofs of his affection, one of the last being a donation of 500 last year towards its redecoration.
10020-8{_}1853{_}Not a great many laymen know the College by sight.
10020-8{_}1854{_}It is a corner building in Trafalgar Square, the entrance facing Whitcomb Street.
10020-8{_}1855{_}The meetings of the Fellows are held in the magnificent library, lined with 60,000 volumes, chiefly classics.
10020-8{_}1856{_}Opening out of the library is the Censors' room, panelled with old oak, and hung with portraits of former Presidents, chiefly by old masters.
10020-8{_}1857{_}At an examination the President sits at the end of the table with his back to the fireplace, the Registrar (Dr. Liveing) opposite, and the Censors on either side.
10020-8{_}1858{_}In front of the President is a cushion with the Caduceus, the Mace, and the Golden Cane.
10020-8{_}1859{_}It was in the library that Sir Andrew presided at the Harveian Oration the day before he was taken ill.  Sir Andrew could not be judged of by the surface.
10020-8{_}1860{_}As Sir Joseph Phayres truly says: "I have known him intimately, and the more I knew him the more I respected and admired him."
10020-8{_}1861{_}Those who knew him best loved him best.
10020-8{_}1862{_}One has only to read how one leading man after another writes of him with enthusiastic appreciation (in the _Medical Journal_) to learn what his colleagues thought of his medical skill and personal character.
10020-8{_}1863{_}A bishop recently spoke of him as the truthful doctor: and a young girl, who from a small child had stayed with him, told me he would always correct himself if he had told an anecdote the least inaccurately; and one day this summer when walking round their garden with him she said the caterpillars had eaten all their gooseberry trees; "I mean the gooseberry _leaves_," she added.
10020-8{_}1864{_}Sir Andrew immediately said, "I am glad you are particular to say what is exactly true"; but, she added, there was always _something_ to remember in everything he said.
10020-8{_}1865{_}With regard to another point, a clergyman who knew Sir Andrew very intimately once told me that "No man of this century had a more keenly religious mind; he was so saturated with thoughts of God and so convinced that God had spoken to man.
10020-8{_}1866{_}He was intensely religious, with a profound sense of the supernatural; he certainly was a great example to very busy men in the way he always managed to find time for church, and even when called away to a distance he would, if possible, go to a church near where he happened to be."
10020-8{_}1867{_}In addition to these qualities, he was very just, sympathetic, and generous.
10020-8{_}1868{_}[Illustration: CAMFIELD HOUSE, ESSENDON.
10020-8{_}1869{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}1870{_}by Mavor & Meredith._]  I have come across many friends who knew him well, and it is interesting to note that the same cardinal points seem to have struck everyone as the key-notes of his life.
10020-8{_}1871{_}In almost identical words each one speaks of his strong faith, his strict veracity, and his intense devotion to duty.
10020-8{_}1872{_}One of his old friends said to me the other day: "_Nothing_ would tempt Clark away from what he thought right; his conscientiousness was unbounded."
10020-8{_}1873{_}His love of metaphysics, combined with a very high motive, made him naturally interested in the _whole_ man--body, mind, and spirit.
10020-8{_}1874{_}To quote the words of a well-known bishop: "It was his intrepid honesty which was so valuable a quality.
10020-8{_}1875{_}In Sir Andrew Clark men felt that he wished to do them good, and to do them the best good, by making men of them."
10020-8{_}1876{_}[Illustration: SIR ANDREW CLARK'S HOUSE IN CAVENDISH SQUARE.
10020-8{_}1877{_}_From a Photograph by Mavor & Meredith._]  The bishop told me a characteristic anecdote illustrating this: "A clergyman complained to him of feeling low and depressed, unable to face his work, and tempted to rely on stimulants.
10020-8{_}1878{_}Sir Andrew saw that the position was a perilous one, and that it was a crisis in the man's life.
10020-8{_}1879{_}He dealt with the case, and forbade resort to stimulants, when the patient declared that he would be unequal to his work and ready to sink.
10020-8{_}1880{_}'Then,' said Sir Andrew, 'sink like a man!'"
10020-8{_}1881{_}This is but one of many incidents showing his marvellous power in restraining his patients and raising them to a higher moral level.
10020-8{_}1882{_}The writer could tell a far more wonderful story of the saving of a drunkard, body and soul, but it is too touching and sacred for publication.
10020-8{_}1883{_}At the top of the wall of that well-known consulting-room (in which Sir Andrew is said to have seen 10,000 patients annually), immediately facing the chair where he always sat, are the words: "Glory to God."
10020-8{_}1884{_}[Illustration: CENSORS' ROOM--COLLEGE OF PHYSICIANS.
10020-8{_}1885{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}1886{_}by Mavor & Meredith_.]
10020-8{_}1887{_}With regard to his profession he was an enthusiast.
10020-8{_}1888{_}He termed medicine "the metropolis of the kingdom of knowledge," and in one of his addresses to students, said: "You have chosen one of the noblest, the most important, and the most interesting of professions, but also the most arduous and the most self-denying, involving the largest sacrifices and the fewest rewards.
10020-8{_}1889{_}He who is not prepared to find in its cultivation and exercise his chief recompense, has mistaken his calling and should retrace his steps."
10020-8{_}1890{_}He had an ideal, and he did his utmost to live up to it.
10020-8{_}1891{_}His words in many instances did as much good as his medicine.
10020-8{_}1892{_}To explain what I mean I cannot do better than quote part of a letter received since Sir Andrew's death, from a delicate, hardworking clergyman, whom I have known some years.
10020-8{_}1893{_}After speaking of Sir Andrew's painstaking kindness, "never seeming the least hurried," he says: "He had a wonderful way of inspiring one with confidence and readiness to face one's troubles.
10020-8{_}1894{_}I remember his saying once, 'It is wonderful how we get _accustomed_ to our troubles,' and at another time, while encouraging me to go on with work--reading for Orders: 'If one is to die, it is better to die doing something, than doing nothing.'
10020-8{_}1895{_}I have often found that a help when feeling done-up and useless.
10020-8{_}1896{_}In the old days when people used to go and see him without an appointment, I have often sat for hours in his dining-room, feeling so ill that I felt as if I should die before I saw him, but after having seen him I felt as if I had got a new lease of life.
10020-8{_}1897{_}I was not at all hypochondriacal or fanciful, I think, but that was the moral effect of an interview with him.
10020-8{_}1898{_}I believe he revolutionized the treatment of cases like mine, and that he, to a certain extent, experimented on me; at any rate, he treated me on philosophical principles, and told me often" (he went to him for twenty years) "that I had become much stronger than he had expected.
10020-8{_}1899{_}He said to me several times: 'You are a wonderful man; you have saved many lives.'"
10020-8{_}1900{_}[Illustration: ENTRANCE HALL--COLLEGE OF PHYSICIANS.
10020-8{_}1901{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}1902{_}by Mavor & Meredith_]  This my correspondent understood to mean the experiments had been successful.
10020-8{_}1903{_}"He once said that if I had died at that time, there was not a doctor in London would have approved of his treatment.
10020-8{_}1904{_}He gave a description of my case some years ago, in a lecture I think at Brighton--but of course without the name.
10020-8{_}1905{_}The particular weakness was valvular disease of the heart, the consequence of rheumatic fever, and this treatment was founded on the principle that Nature always works towards compensation.
10020-8{_}1906{_}He told me many years ago that that particular mischief was fully compensated for."
10020-8{_}1907{_}[Illustration: THE READING ROOM--COLLEGE OF PHYSICIANS.
10020-8{_}1908{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}1909{_}by Mavor & Meredith_]  He loved his work and never tired of it.
10020-8{_}1910{_}He often told the story how his first serious case, and encouraging cure, was himself.
10020-8{_}1911{_}With severe hemorrhage of the lungs, he was told it would be at the risk of his life if he went on with his studies.
10020-8{_}1912{_}A doctor, however, he made up his mind he would be, and that he would begin by making every effort to cure himself.
10020-8{_}1913{_}With characteristic determination, he persisted in a strict regimen of diet and fresh air.
10020-8{_}1914{_}"I determined," said Sir Andrew, "as far as my studies would allow me--for I never intended to give them up--to live in the fresh air, often studying out of doors; and in a short time I was so much better that I was able to take gentle exercise.
10020-8{_}1915{_}I got well, and I may almost say I got over the trouble which threatened me."
10020-8{_}1916{_}The lungs were healed, and a result which seemed inevitable avoided.
10020-8{_}1917{_}He would often say he obtained his first appointment at the London Hospital chiefly out of pity, the authorities thinking he would not live six months, but he outlived almost every one of them.
10020-8{_}1918{_}[Illustration: THE CADUCENS, MACE, BOOK, AND SEAL--COLLEGE OF PHYSICIANS _From a Photo by Mavor & Meredith_]  No man could have kept on for fourteen and sixteen hours a day, as Sir Andrew did, without unbounded enthusiasm and an absorbing interest.
10020-8{_}1919{_}His enormous correspondence must have been the great tax.
10020-8{_}1920{_}Most people are disinclined to write a dozen letters at the end of a hard day's work; but Sir Andrew often came home at eight o'clock with the knowledge that letters would occupy him until after midnight.
10020-8{_}1921{_}His letters averaged sixty per day.
10020-8{_}1922{_}These would be answered by return, except where minute directions were inclosed.
10020-8{_}1923{_}Only the other day, a friend of his told me, Sir Andrew came in the morning, a short time before he was taken ill, looking very tired and worried.
10020-8{_}1924{_}On being asked the reason, he said he had not slept all night, for he went to see a patient three days before, and because he had not sent the table of directions, the patient wrote saying he would not try his treatment.
10020-8{_}1925{_}"I never slept," said Sir Andrew, "thinking of the state of mind to which I had unavoidably reduced that poor patient."
10020-8{_}1926{_}In order to get through his work he had a light breakfast at 7.30, when he read his letters, which were opened for him.
10020-8{_}1927{_}From eight until two or three he saw patients, his simple luncheon being taken in the consulting-room.
10020-8{_}1928{_}He would then go to the hospital, College of Physicians, or some consultation; he had often after that to go to see someone at a distance, but he never worried a patient by seeming in a hurry, however much pressed for time.
10020-8{_}1929{_}He had a very strong sense of responsibility, and would never rest himself by staying the night if it were unnecessary.
10020-8{_}1930{_}A rich patient in Devonshire once offered him a large sum to stay until the next morning.
10020-8{_}1931{_}"I could do you no good," said Sir Andrew, "and my patients will want me to-morrow."
10020-8{_}1932{_}Among his patients were almost all the great authors, philosophers, and intellectual men of the day.
10020-8{_}1933{_}Longfellow, Tennyson, Huxley, Cardinal Manning, and numerous others were his warm friends.
10020-8{_}1934{_}He always declared he caught many a cold in the ascetic Cardinal's "cold house."
10020-8{_}1935{_}An old pupil truly says Sir Andrew had the rare faculty of surveying the conditions and circumstances of each one, gathering them up, and clearly seeing what was best to do.
10020-8{_}1936{_}Professor Sheridan Delapine says: "He was specially fond of quoting Sydenham's words: 'Tota ars medici est in observationibus.'"
10020-8{_}1937{_}After asking what was amiss and questioning them on what they told him, he would say: "Give me a plan of your day.
10020-8{_}1938{_}What is your work?
10020-8{_}1939{_}When do you take your meals?
10020-8{_}1940{_}Of what do they consist?
10020-8{_}1941{_}What time do you get up, and when do you go to bed?"
10020-8{_}1942{_}Notwithstanding the keenness of his eye and natural intuition, which found out instantly far more than was told, he not only eagerly and attentively listened, but _remembered_ what his patient said.
10020-8{_}1943{_}Sir Henry Roscoe gave me a striking instance of this, and I cannot do better than quote his exact words:--  "I first made Sir Andrew's acquaintance about twenty years ago at Braemar, where he was spending the autumn, and, as was his kindly wont, had with him a young Manchester man, far gone in consumption, to whom he acted as friend, counsellor, and physician.
10020-8{_}1944{_}In our frequent walks and talks, I confided in the eminent doctor that I had suffered from that frequent plague of sedentary men, the gout.
10020-8{_}1945{_}'Come and see me any morning in Cavendish Square before eight,' said he, 'and I will do what I can for you.'
10020-8{_}1946{_}Many years slipped by; living then in Manchester, I never took advantage of the kind offer, and I never saw Sir Andrew until some eight years afterwards.
10020-8{_}1947{_}I was calling on my old friend, Sir Joseph Whitworth, who at that time had rooms in Great George Street.
10020-8{_}1948{_}As I came quickly out of the front door, Clark's carriage drove up, and almost before it stopped the Doctor 'bounced' out and we nearly ran against each other.
10020-8{_}1949{_}In one 'instant-minute,' as our American friends say, he accosted me: 'Well!
10020-8{_}1950{_}How's the gout?'
10020-8{_}1951{_}He had no more idea of meeting me at that moment than of meeting the man in the moon, and yet, no sooner had he seen my face--which he had not looked upon for eight years--than the whole 'case' flashed upon him.
10020-8{_}1952{_}Since that time I have often seen him, and I shall always retain not only a high opinion of his great gifts, but also an affectionate remembrance of his great-heartedness."
10020-8{_}1953{_}Literary people and brain-workers particularly interested him, and they found in the kind doctor a friend who understood them.
10020-8{_}1954{_}He would advise all writing that involved thought to be done in the morning before luncheon.
10020-8{_}1955{_}The evening might be spent in "taking in" or reading up the subject of a book or paper, but there must be no giving out.
10020-8{_}1956{_}For brain-workers who were not strong, he insisted on meat in the middle of the day; he declared that for this class it was "physiologically wicked" even to have luncheon without.
10020-8{_}1957{_}To one who spoke of fatigue after a comparatively short walk, he replied: "Walk little, then.
10020-8{_}1958{_}Many who work their brain are not up to much exercise.
10020-8{_}1959{_}I hardly ever walk a mile myself; but that need not prevent men having plenty of fresh air."
10020-8{_}1960{_}[Illustration: THE LONDON HOSPITAL _From a Photo.
10020-8{_}1961{_}by Mavor & Meredith._]  Some people laugh at his rules for diet, etc., forgetting that these simple directions are based on deep knowledge of the human frame.
10020-8{_}1962{_}Let them laugh.
10020-8{_}1963{_}Many who have tried them know they have been different people in consequence.
10020-8{_}1964{_}His incisive words--"My friend, you eat too much!"
10020-8{_}1965{_}"My friend, you drink too much!"
10020-8{_}1966{_}would not he appreciated by all; but Sir Andrew thought nearly all diseases were the outcome of the constant and apparently unimportant violation of the laws of health.
10020-8{_}1967{_}Those who were hopelessly ill would always hear the truth from him, but he would leave no stone unturned to lessen their suffering.
10020-8{_}1968{_}Many an incurable patient has he sent to a home from the London Hospital, and visited them afterwards.
10020-8{_}1969{_}Only the other day I heard of patients he had sent to St. Elizabeth's, Great Ormond Street, where incurable patients are nursed and cared for until they die, and never left the hospital without leaving a guinea with one of the nuns.
10020-8{_}1970{_}Sir Andrew had no stereotyped plan.
10020-8{_}1971{_}It was not merely the disease, but the individual he treated.
10020-8{_}1972{_}A friend told me he saved her aunt's life.
10020-8{_}1973{_}She could not sleep, and Sir Andrew ordered them to give her breakfast at five, "for after tossing about all night she might sleep after having some food," and so it proved.
10020-8{_}1974{_}[Illustration: THE HARRISON WARD--LONDON HOSPITAL.
10020-8{_}1975{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}1976{_}by Mavor & Meredith._]  To others who might get well, he would say: "Fight for your life."
10020-8{_}1977{_}Twelve years ago a lady (whom I met lately) had hemorrhage of the lungs three times.
10020-8{_}1978{_}She was told by seven doctors in the country that she "had not a week to live."
10020-8{_}1979{_}She had young children, and determined to make a great effort to see Sir Andrew Clark.
10020-8{_}1980{_}He prophesied she would get well, providing she at once left the damp climate where she was then living and made her permanent home at Malvern.
10020-8{_}1981{_}A week after she had taken his remedies she walked up the Wrekin.
10020-8{_}1982{_}From that day she saw Sir Andrew once every year, and looks upon herself as a monument of his skill.
10020-8{_}1983{_}"Die to live," was a favourite saying of Sir Andrew's.
10020-8{_}1984{_}"In congenial work you will find life, strength, and happiness."
10020-8{_}1985{_}This certainly was his own experience.
10020-8{_}1986{_}Only in July last he said to the writer of this notice: "I never know what it is to feel well now, but work is the joy of my life."
10020-8{_}1987{_}He could, however, place strict limits as to how much a _patient_ might work.
10020-8{_}1988{_}It is well known how docile and obedient a patient he had in Mr. Gladstone.
10020-8{_}1989{_}One evening, coming downstairs muffled up to avoid a worse cold, he was met by Sir Andrew with the greeting, "Where are you going?"
10020-8{_}1990{_}"To the House," said Mr. Gladstone.
10020-8{_}1991{_}"No, you are not," replied his friend; "you are going straight to bed!"
10020-8{_}1992{_}and to bed he went.
10020-8{_}1993{_}Sir Andrew also limited the time Mr. Gladstone should speak.
10020-8{_}1994{_}On one occasion, however, notwithstanding the fact that the peremptory adviser was present, watch in hand, Mr. Gladstone, after throwing down the written speech as the clock struck, went on for another half-hour!
10020-8{_}1995{_}[A] This disobedience was the exception which proved the rule.
10020-8{_}1996{_}[A] The substance of this anecdote which I quote from memory,     appeared in the _Daily News_, and happened at Newcastle.
10020-8{_}1997{_}Mr. Gladstone was a friend for whom Sir Andrew had the highest respect and veneration, and hardly ever passed a day without going to see him.
10020-8{_}1998{_}Shortly before he was taken ill he said: "For twenty years I have never heard Gladstone say an unkind or vituperative word of anyone."
10020-8{_}1999{_}[Illustration: NURSE HARRISON--LONDON HOSPITAL.
10020-8{_}2000{_}(The nurse who tended Sir Andrew Clark in his last illness.)
10020-8{_}2001{_}_From a Photograph by Mavor & Meredith._]  With respect to fees, he always took what was offered: sometimes he would receive 500 for a long journey, sometimes two guineas.
10020-8{_}2002{_}The following is no doubt but one of many similar experiences.
10020-8{_}2003{_}After a hard day's work he was urgently summoned to a place 120 miles from London.
10020-8{_}2004{_}It was a very wet night.
10020-8{_}2005{_}There was no carriage to meet him; no fly to be had.
10020-8{_}2006{_}After walking a mile or two he arrived at a small farm, and found the daughter suffering from an attack of hysteria.
10020-8{_}2007{_}Sir Andrew, with his usual kindness, did what he could and evidently gave satisfaction, for when he left the mother said: "Well, Sir Andrew, you have been so kind we must make it double," and handed him two guineas.
10020-8{_}2008{_}He thanked them and said: "Good-bye."
10020-8{_}2009{_}Sir Andrew would never hear of charging more than his usual fee because a person happened to be very rich.
10020-8{_}2010{_}In a word, he was honest.
10020-8{_}2011{_}On one occasion when going to see a patient in the south, the doctor who was to meet him in consultation met Sir Andrew at the station, told him they were rich, and quite prepared to pay a very high fee.
10020-8{_}2012{_}But Sir Andrew replied: "I did not come from London," and naming the place where he was staying, said, "My fee is only a third of the sum you name."
10020-8{_}2013{_}Sir Andrew was not indifferent to fees; on the contrary, he rather took a pride in telling how much he earned.
10020-8{_}2014{_}He is said to have once received 5,000 for going to Cannes, the largest _medical_ fee known.
10020-8{_}2015{_}Some, however, have wondered who did pay him--so numerous were his non-paying patients.
10020-8{_}2016{_}From Anglican and Roman Catholic clergy, sisters, nuns, and all engaged in any charitable work (unless rich men) he would never consent to receive a fee, at the same time making it felt that unwillingness to accept his advice "would deprive him of a pleasure"; and it was felt that this was literally true, and if anything the patients whom he saw "as a friend" were shown more consideration than others.
10020-8{_}2017{_}"Come and see me next week," he said to one who demurred to the necessity for going again, knowing he would not accept a fee, "and I will arrange that you shall not be kept waiting."
10020-8{_}2018{_}[Illustration: FACSIMILE OF A PRESCRIPTION WRITTEN BY SIR ANDREW CLARK.]
10020-8{_}2019{_}The present Lord Tennyson writes: "We are among the many who are much indebted to Sir Andrew Clark.
10020-8{_}2020{_}It was in a great measure owing to him that my father recovered from his dangerous attack of gout in 1888, when 'he was as near death as a man could be.'
10020-8{_}2021{_}After this illness Sir Andrew paid us a visit, at Aldworth, in the summer of 1889.
10020-8{_}2022{_}He told us that he had come in spite of a summons from the Shah, to which he had replied that the Shah's Hakim could not obey, as he had promised to visit his old friend--the old Poet.
10020-8{_}2023{_}Sir Andrew added: 'This disobedience of your humble and devoted physician for the sake of his friend, the crowned King of Song, struck the crowned King of Kings so much that, so far from being offended, he took a noble view, and, as a mark of signal honour, sent me the Star of the Second Class of the Lion and Sun of Persia.'"
10020-8{_}2024{_}[Illustration: SIR JAMES CLARK.
10020-8{_}2025{_}(Eldest son of Sir Andrew Clark.)
10020-8{_}2026{_}_From a Photograph by Wyrall, Aldershot._]  Sundays were often spent out of town, at Hawarden and elsewhere, and latterly at Camfield, the house so lately purchased.
10020-8{_}2027{_}Both this and his town house were entirely furnished, as he wished each to be complete in itself.
10020-8{_}2028{_}Already at Essendon the example of his life was felt to be a power for good, as well as the kind interest he took in his poorer neighbours, inviting them up to his house, promising to give the men a dinner at Christmas, etc.
10020-8{_}2029{_}Yet Sir Andrew was no "country gentleman"; his favourite recreation was books.
10020-8{_}2030{_}On being asked: "Which way are we looking?
10020-8{_}2031{_}In which direction is London?"
10020-8{_}2032{_}he replied: "I don't know."
10020-8{_}2033{_}"Don't you know how the house stands, or what soil it is built upon?"
10020-8{_}2034{_}and again he had to plead ignorance.
10020-8{_}2035{_}Nevertheless, his love of neatness made him notice if a place was in good order.
10020-8{_}2036{_}One day, driving over to see some neighbours, after congratulating them on the well-kept garden, he was getting into the carriage, when he suddenly remembered he had not told the gardener how much pleased he was with the whole place, and with his usual courtesy insisted on going back to find him.
10020-8{_}2037{_}One of Sir Andrew's holidays was a trip to Canada, when he accompanied the Marquis of Lorne and Princess Louise, on the former being appointed Governor-General there.
10020-8{_}2038{_}This he did as a friend, and in no way in a medical capacity.
10020-8{_}2039{_}He was most popular on the voyage out among the passengers, keeping the ship alive with jokes and amusing stories, and many called him "Merry Andrew."
10020-8{_}2040{_}He was almost boyish in his keen enjoyment of a holiday.
10020-8{_}2041{_}He was evidently devoted to music, and was delighted with the beautiful string band the Duke of Edinburgh brought on board at Halifax.
10020-8{_}2042{_}In Canada, Sir Andrew was most warmly received and universally liked by everyone.
10020-8{_}2043{_}Amongst others he made the acquaintance of Sir John Macdonald.
10020-8{_}2044{_}The Princess told me without doubt there was one predominating interest in his mind, and that the supernatural--whether at a British Association meeting, the College of Physicians, or speaking privately to his own friends.
10020-8{_}2045{_}He realized the impossibility of explaining by scientific methods the supernatural.
10020-8{_}2046{_}He would often say: "There is more in Heaven and earth than this world dreams of.
10020-8{_}2047{_}Given the most _perfect_ scientific methods, you will find beyond abysses which you are powerless to explore."
10020-8{_}2048{_}He had the greatest charm of mind, and, needless to say, was a delightful companion.
10020-8{_}2049{_}His topics of conversation were extremely varied: he liked dialectics for talk and argument's sake, and enjoyed talking to those who had somewhat the same taste.
10020-8{_}2050{_}Possibly for this reason he did not fully appreciate children, although they amused him, and he liked to understand their ideas.
10020-8{_}2051{_}A friend of Sir Andrew's staying with him at the time told me the following characteristic anecdote: One afternoon during his autumn holiday in Scotland the footman came in to put coals on the fire, and a child (a relation) coughed vehemently.
10020-8{_}2052{_}"Why do you cough so much?"
10020-8{_}2053{_}said Sir Andrew.
10020-8{_}2054{_}"To make James look at me," said the child.
10020-8{_}2055{_}Sir Andrew was "solemnly interested," and afterwards took it as a parable of a woman's nature, which, speaking generally, he considered morally and ethically inferior to a man's.
10020-8{_}2056{_}In his opinion very many women were wanting in the two great qualities--justice and truth--considering their own, their children's, or their husband's interests first rather than what was absolutely right.
10020-8{_}2057{_}One subject that interested him very much was heredity, and he had, of course, countless opportunities of studying it.
10020-8{_}2058{_}"Temperance and morality," he would say, "are most distinctly transmitted, especially by the mother; but," said Sir Andrew, "in spite of heredity, I am what I am by my own choice."
10020-8{_}2059{_}Sir Andrew was a great reader.
10020-8{_}2060{_}Metaphysics, philosophy, and theology were his favourite subjects, especially the latter--he also occasionally read a good novel.
10020-8{_}2061{_}Reading was his only relaxation, for it was one he could enjoy while driving or in the train.
10020-8{_}2062{_}Dr. Russell, who was with him when going to attend the tercentenary of Dublin College, tells the story how Sir Andrew not only read but wrote hour after hour in the railway carriage, and, in addition, listened to the conversation.
10020-8{_}2063{_}Dr. Russell Reynolds, Sir James Paget, Sir Dyce Duckworth, and Sir R. Quain were of the party, and the two latter joined Dr. Russell in remarking with him that it would ruin his eyesight.
10020-8{_}2064{_}"I am using my eyes, not abusing them," replied Sir Andrew; "you cannot injure any organ by the exercise of it, but by the excess of exercise of it.
10020-8{_}2065{_}I would not do it were I not accustomed to read and write without the smallest amount of mischief."
10020-8{_}2066{_}I much regret that lack of space prevents my describing the London Hospital as I should like.
10020-8{_}2067{_}Of most hospitals Sir Andrew was a governor, but his great interest was the London, of which he and Lady Clark were both life governors.
10020-8{_}2068{_}While Sir Andrew was visiting physician he came regularly twice a week, as well as for consultation.
10020-8{_}2069{_}He was interested in everything that concerned the patients, and always had a kind word for the nurses.
10020-8{_}2070{_}One nurse in the Charlotte Ward (Sir Andrew Clark's) said he used literally to shovel out half-crowns at Christmas when he asked what the patients were going to do.
10020-8{_}2071{_}Everyone speaks Of the pecuniary sacrifice and strain his connection with the hospital involved.
10020-8{_}2072{_}He endowed a medical tutorship, also scholarships for students.
10020-8{_}2073{_}Students, nurses, etc., would eagerly listen to his informal expositions in the wards, as he invariably showed a grasp of the subject that was equally minute and comprehensive.
10020-8{_}2074{_}"He would start from some particular point and work his way point by point down to the minutest detail, not bewildering by a multiplicity of facts, but keeping them all in order with perfect handling, until the framing of the whole thing stood out luminously clear to the dullest comprehension.
10020-8{_}2075{_}An old pupil says his well-known authoritative manner was the result of a profound and laboriously acquired knowledge of his art, acquired by years of careful work in hospital wards and post-mortem rooms."
10020-8{_}2076{_}--_Medical Journal_.
10020-8{_}2077{_}[Illustration: SIR ANDREW CLARK.
10020-8{_}2078{_}_From a Painting by G.F. Watts, R.A._]  Happily there are two portraits of Sir Andrew.
10020-8{_}2079{_}The last beautifully painted picture by Mr. Watts (which by the great kindness of the artist is allowed to be reproduced in this sketch) was only finished a few days before Sir Andrew was taken ill--for he could only sit from eight till nine a.m.
10020-8{_}2080{_}It is one of the series Mr. Watts is so generously giving to the nation, and he "thinks it one of his best."
10020-8{_}2081{_}Sir Andrew himself was delighted with it, saying in his hearty way to Mrs. Watts: "Why, it _thinks_!"
10020-8{_}2082{_}The position in the picture by Frank Holl is unfortunate.
10020-8{_}2083{_}Very imperfectly I have described the varied work of a man of limitless energy, with an exceptionally keen appreciation of men and things.
10020-8{_}2084{_}A great man has passed away, and we are poorer in consequence.
10020-8{_}2085{_}*       *       *       *       *    _Beauties:--Children._   [Illustration: Winnifred Emma Heale.
10020-8{_}2086{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}2087{_}by Heath & Bradnee, Exeter._]  [Illustration: Edith Marguerite Dickinson.
10020-8{_}2088{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}2089{_}by J. Hargreaves, Barrow-in-Furness._]  [Illustration: Myrta Vivienne Stubbs.
10020-8{_}2090{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}2091{_}by Medringtons, Ltd., Liverpool._]  [Illustration: Kathleen Keyse _From a Photograph._]  [Illustration: Madge Erskine _From a Photo.
10020-8{_}2092{_}by Allison & Allison, Belfast._]  [Illustration: Dorothy Birch Done _From a Photo.
10020-8{_}2093{_}by Stanley Hurst, Wrexham._]  [Illustration: Evelyn Mary Dowdell.
10020-8{_}2094{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}2095{_}by G. Ridsdale Cleare, Lower Clapton, N. E._]  [Illustration: Nelly M. Morris.
10020-8{_}2096{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}2097{_}by J. W. Thomas, Colwyn Bay._]  [Illustration: Aligander Smith.
10020-8{_}2098{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}2099{_}by Norman, May, & Co., Ltd., Malvern._]         *       *       *       *       *    _The Signatures of Charles Dickens (with Portraits)._  FROM 1825 TO 1870.
10020-8{_}2100{_}(Born 7th February, 1812; died 9th June, 1870.)
10020-8{_}2101{_}BY J. HOLT SCHOOLING.
10020-8{_}2102{_}"Everybody knows what Dickens's signature is like"--says the reader who bases acquaintance with it upon the familiar, gold-impressed facsimile on the well-known red covers of his works--"a free, dashing signature, with an extensive and well-graduated flourish underneath."
10020-8{_}2103{_}(No.
10020-8{_}2104{_}1.)
10020-8{_}2105{_}Aye!
10020-8{_}2106{_}But have you ever seen an original Dickens-letter?
10020-8{_}2107{_}Have you ever handled, not one, but hundreds of his documents--letters, franked envelopes, cheques signed by Dickens, cheques indorsed by him, legal agreements bearing his signature, and the original MSS.
10020-8{_}2108{_}of his works?
10020-8{_}2109{_}Owing to the kindness of owners and guardians of Dickens-letters, etc.
10020-8{_}2110{_}I have been able to supplement the materials in my own collection by numerous facsimiles taken direct from a priceless store of Dickens-MSS.
10020-8{_}2111{_}Here are some of the specimens.
10020-8{_}2112{_}We will glance over them, and in doing so will view them, not merely as signatures, but also as permanently-recorded tracings of Dickens's nerve muscular action--of his _gesture_.
10020-8{_}2113{_}The expressive play of his facial muscles has gone, the varying inflections of voice have gone, but we still possess the self-registered and characteristic tracings of Charles Dickens's hand-gesture.
10020-8{_}2114{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2115{_}1.--FAMILIAR "BOOK COVER" SIGNATURE.]
10020-8{_}2116{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2117{_}2.--WRITTEN IN 1825.]
10020-8{_}2118{_}In No.
10020-8{_}2119{_}1 we have the signature of Dickens as he wrote it when aged forty-five to fifty; in No.
10020-8{_}2120{_}2 there is the boy's signature at the age of thirteen, written to a school-fellow.
10020-8{_}2121{_}This youthful signature shows the existence in embryo form of the "flourish" so commonly associated with Dickens's signature.
10020-8{_}2122{_}It is interesting to note that the receiver of this early letter has stated that its schoolboy writer had "more than usual flow of spirits, held his head more erect than lads ordinarily do," and that "there was a general smartness about him."
10020-8{_}2123{_}We shall perhaps see that the direct emphasis of so many of Charles Dickens's signatures which is given by his "flourish" may be fitly associated with certain characteristics of the man himself.
10020-8{_}2124{_}We may also note that high spirits and vigorous nervous energy are productive of redundant nerve-muscular activity in any direction--hand gesture included.
10020-8{_}2125{_}[Illustration: AGE 18.
10020-8{_}2126{_}_From a Miniature by Mrs. Janet Barrow_.]
10020-8{_}2127{_}Let us look at some other early signatures.
10020-8{_}2128{_}Hitherto they have been stowed away in various collections, and they are almost unknown.
10020-8{_}2129{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2130{_}3.--WRITTEN IN 1830.]
10020-8{_}2131{_}The next facsimile, No.
10020-8{_}2132{_}3, is remarkable as being almost the only full signature out of hundreds I have seen which lacks the flourish; this specimen is also worth notice, owing to the "droop" of every word below the horizontal level from which each starts--a little piece of nerve-muscular evidence of mental or physical depression, which may be tested by anyone who cares to examine his own handwriting produced under conditions which diminish bodily vigour or mental _lan_.
10020-8{_}2133{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2134{_}4.--WRITTEN IN 1831.]
10020-8{_}2135{_}The writing of No.
10020-8{_}2136{_}4 is very like that of No.
10020-8{_}2137{_}3; the easy curves below the signature are cleverly made, and while they indicate much energy, they also point to a useful confidence in self, owing to the deliberate way of accentuating the most personal part of a letter--its signature.
10020-8{_}2138{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2139{_}5.--WRITTEN IN 1832.]
10020-8{_}2140{_}No.
10020-8{_}2141{_}5 is the facsimile of a signature to a letter which was written in the Library of the British Museum to "My dear Knolle"; the letter ends: "Believe me (in haste), yours most truly."
10020-8{_}2142{_}At this time--1832--Dickens was a newspaper reporter, and it is curious to notice that in spite of "haste" he yet managed to execute this complex movement underneath the signature.
10020-8{_}2143{_}Its force and energy are great, but we shall see even more pronounced developments of this flourish before it takes the moderated and graceful form of confident and assured power.
10020-8{_}2144{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2145{_}6.--WRITTEN IN 1833 OR 1834.]
10020-8{_}2146{_}There is still more force and "go" about No.
10020-8{_}2147{_}6: it was written on "Wednesday night, past 12," and also in haste.
10020-8{_}2148{_}Dickens was reporting for the _Morning Chronicle_, and was just starting on a journey, but yet there are here two separate flourishes; one begins under the _s_ of _Charles_ and ends under the _C_ of that name; the other starts under the capital _D_ and finishes below the _n_ of _Dickens_.
10020-8{_}2149{_}[Illustration: AGE 23.
10020-8{_}2150{_}_From a Miniature by Miss R. E. Drummond._]  [Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2151{_}7.--WRITTEN IN 1836.]
10020-8{_}2152{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2153{_}8.--WRITTEN OCT. 1, 1836.]
10020-8{_}2154{_}The intricacy of the next facsimile, No.
10020-8{_}2155{_}7, is an ugly but a very active piece of movement.
10020-8{_}2156{_}This group of curves is equal to about a two-feet length of pen-stroke, a fact which indicates an extraordinary amount of personal energy.
10020-8{_}2157{_}Dickens was then writing his "Sketches by Boz," and this ungraceful elaboration of his signature was probably accompanied by a growing sense of his own capacity and power.
10020-8{_}2158{_}During the time-interval between the signatures shown in Nos.
10020-8{_}2159{_}7 and 8, the first number of the "Pickwick Papers" was published--March, 1836--and Charles Dickens married Catherine Hogarth on the 2nd of April in that year.
10020-8{_}2160{_}The original of a very different facsimile (No.
10020-8{_}2161{_}9) was written as a receipt in the account-book of Messrs. Chapman and Hall for an advance of 5.
10020-8{_}2162{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2163{_}9.--WRITTEN IN 1837.]
10020-8{_}2164{_}The six facsimiles numbered 9 to 15 deserve special notice.
10020-8{_}2165{_}The originals were all written in the year 1837, and I have purposely shown them because their extraordinary variations entirely negative the popular idea about the uniformity of Dickens's handwriting, and because these mobile hand-gestures are a striking illustration of the mobility and great sensibility to impressions which were prominent features in Charles Dickens's nature.
10020-8{_}2166{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2167{_}10.--WRITTEN IN 1837.]
10020-8{_}2168{_}Common observation show us that a man whose mind is specially receptive of impressions from persons and things around him, and whose sensibility is very quick, can scarcely fail to show much variation in his own forms of outward expression--such, for example, as facial "play," voice-inflections, hand-gestures, and so on.
10020-8{_}2169{_}Notice the originality in the position of the flourishes shown in No.
10020-8{_}2170{_}9, and compare the ungraceful movement of it with the much more dignified and pleasing flourishes in some of the later signatures.
10020-8{_}2171{_}A whimsical originality of mind comes out also in the curious "B" of "Boz" (No.
10020-8{_}2172{_}10).
10020-8{_}2173{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2174{_}11.--WRITTEN NOV. 3, 1837.]
10020-8{_}2175{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2176{_}12.--WRITTEN NOV. 3, 1837.]
10020-8{_}2177{_}[Illustration: AGE 25.
10020-8{_}2178{_}_From a Drawing by H. K. Browne._]  The next pair--Nos.
10020-8{_}2179{_}11 and 12--are interesting.
10020-8{_}2180{_}No.
10020-8{_}2181{_}11 shows the signature squeezed in at the bottom of a page; the flourish was attempted, and accompanied by the words: "No room for the flouish," the _r_ of _flourish_ being omitted.
10020-8{_}2182{_}No.
10020-8{_}2183{_}12 was written on the envelope of the same letter.
10020-8{_}2184{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2185{_}l3.--WRITTEN NOV. 18, 1837.
10020-8{_}2186{_}_Taken from the Legal Agreement re "Pickwick.
10020-8{_}2187{_}"_]  [Illustration: AGE 29.
10020-8{_}2188{_}_From a Drawing by Alfred Count D'Orsay._]  No.
10020-8{_}2189{_}13 is a copy of a very famous signature: the original is on a great parchment called "Deed of License Assignment and Covenants respecting a Work called 'The Pickwick Papers,'" and which, after a preamble, contains the words: "Whereas the said Charles Dickens is the Author of a Book or Work intituled 'The Posthumous Papers of the Pickwick Club,' which has been recently printed and published in twenty parts or numbers," etc.
10020-8{_}2190{_}It is probable that the fact of the seal being placed between _Charles_ and _Dickens_ prevented the flourish which almost invariably accompanied his signatures on business documents; the marked enlargement of this signature takes the place of the flourish, and shows an unconscious emphasis of the _ego_.
10020-8{_}2191{_}It would be almost unreasonable for us to expect that so impressionable a man, who was also feeling his power and fame, could abstain from showing outward signs of his own consciousness of abnormal success.
10020-8{_}2192{_}Yet, in the private letters of Dickens, the simple "C. D." is very frequent; a few examples of it are given in this article, and their present number in no way represents the numerical relation of these simple signatures to the more "showy" ones.
10020-8{_}2193{_}It may at once be said that this point of difference is alike interesting to the student of gesture and to the student of Dickens's character.
10020-8{_}2194{_}He was certainly a very able man of business, and the wording of his "business" letters fully bears out the idea conveyed by his "business" signature--so to speak--that Dickens was fully aware of his own powers, and that, quite fairly, he did not omit to impress the fact upon other people when he thought fit.
10020-8{_}2195{_}Both the wording and the signature of many of his private letters are simple and unostentatious to a high degree.
10020-8{_}2196{_}This curious fact, which is now illustrated by Charles Dickens's own hand-gesture, ought to be remembered when people talk about Dickens's "conceit" and "love of show."
10020-8{_}2197{_}My explanation is, I think, both logical and true.
10020-8{_}2198{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2199{_}14.--WRITTEN IN 1837.]
10020-8{_}2200{_}[Illustration: AGE 30.
10020-8{_}2201{_}_From a Portrait-Bust by H. Dexter._]  No.
10020-8{_}2202{_}14 closes this series for the year 1837.
10020-8{_}2203{_}It shows a quaint and pretty signature on a wrapper.
10020-8{_}2204{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2205{_}15.--WRITTEN MARCH 12, 1841.
10020-8{_}2206{_}_(Announcing the Death of "Raven", a prominent character in "Barnaby Rudge")_]  [Illustration: AGE ABOUT 30.
10020-8{_}2207{_}_From a Drawing by R.J. Lane, A.E._]  No.
10020-8{_}2208{_}15 shows part of a very humorous and famous letter announcing the death of the raven which figures in "Barnaby Rudge."
10020-8{_}2209{_}Notice the curious originality of form shown in the capital _Y_ and _R_.
10020-8{_}2210{_}The wording of this letter is also quaintly original, and the sensitive mind of this man again caused his nerve-muscular action--his gesture--to harmonize with his mood.
10020-8{_}2211{_}Points of this kind, which the handwriting of Dickens illustrates so well, have a deeper meaning for the observant than for the casual reader of a magazine article; they indicate that these little human acts, which have been so long overlooked by intelligent men, do really give us valuable data for the study of mind by means of written-gesture.
10020-8{_}2212{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2213{_}16.--WRITTEN IN 1841]  [Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2214{_}17.--WRITTEN IN 1841.]
10020-8{_}2215{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2216{_}18.--WRITTEN IN 1843.]
10020-8{_}2217{_}[Illustration: CHARLES DICKENS READING "THE CHIMES," 1844.
10020-8{_}2218{_}_From the original Sketch by David Maelise, R.A._]  [Illustration: CHARLES DICKENS AS "CAPTAIN BOBADIL" IN "EVERY MAN IN HIS HUMOUR."
10020-8{_}2219{_}_From a Painting by C.R.
10020-8{_}2220{_}Leslie, R.A._]  In No.
10020-8{_}2221{_}16 we see another and very original form of the "Boz" signature.
10020-8{_}2222{_}No.
10020-8{_}2223{_}17 has a curious stroke of activity above the signature.
10020-8{_}2224{_}No.
10020-8{_}2225{_}18 is a fine, strong signature.
10020-8{_}2226{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2227{_}19.--WRITTEN IN 1845.]
10020-8{_}2228{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2229{_}20.--WRITTEN MAY 12, 1848.
10020-8{_}2230{_}(PASS TO THE STAGE.)]
10020-8{_}2231{_}[Illustration: CHARLES DICKENS AS "SIR CHARLES COLDSTREAM" IN "USED UP", 1850.
10020-8{_}2232{_}_From a Painting by Augustus Egg, R.A._]  No.
10020-8{_}2233{_}19 is remarkably vigorous and active.
10020-8{_}2234{_}The well-controlled activity and energy of the signatures are now strongly marked.
10020-8{_}2235{_}No.
10020-8{_}2236{_}20 explains itself; the curious _P_ of _Pass_ is worth notice.
10020-8{_}2237{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2238{_}21.--WRITTEN JULY 22, 1854.]
10020-8{_}2239{_}[Illustration: CHARLES DICKENS IN HIS STUDY, 1854.
10020-8{_}2240{_}_From the Picture by E.M. Ward, R.A._]  [Illustration: AGE 44.
10020-8{_}2241{_}_From the Painting by Ary Scheffer_.]
10020-8{_}2242{_}No.
10020-8{_}2243{_}21 is a stray illustration of clever and gracefully-executed movements which abound in Dickens's letters.
10020-8{_}2244{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2245{_}22.--WRITTEN WHEN ILL, OCT. 29, 1859]  [Illustration: AGE 47.
10020-8{_}2246{_}_From an Oil Painting by W.P.
10020-8{_}2247{_}Frith, R.A._]  See, in No.
10020-8{_}2248{_}22, how illness disturbed the fine action of this splendid organism; but illness did not prevent attention to detail--the dot is placed after the _D_.
10020-8{_}2249{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2250{_}23.--WRITTEN NOV. 1, 1860.]
10020-8{_}2251{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2252{_}24.--WRITTEN JAN. 17, 1861.]
10020-8{_}2253{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2254{_}25.--WRITTEN NOV. 25, 1861.]
10020-8{_}2255{_}[Illustration: DICKENS AS "RICHARD WARDOUR" IN "THE FROZEN DEEP."]
10020-8{_}2256{_}[Illustration: AGE 49.
10020-8{_}2257{_}_From a Photograph_.]
10020-8{_}2258{_}[Illustration: AGE 51.
10020-8{_}2259{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}2260{_}by Alphonse Maze, Paris._]  When on a reading tour, Dickens wrote at Bideford the letter from which No.
10020-8{_}2261{_}23 has been copied.
10020-8{_}2262{_}After writing that he could get nothing to eat or drink at the small inn, he wrote the sentence facsimiled.
10020-8{_}2263{_}The exaggeration of the words is matched by the use of two capital _T_'s in place of two small _t_'s.
10020-8{_}2264{_}The letter continues: "The landlady is playing cribbage with the landlord in the next room (behind a thin partition), and they seem quite comfortable."
10020-8{_}2265{_}No.
10020-8{_}2266{_}24 is another instance of the variation which, in fact, obtained up to the very day before death.
10020-8{_}2267{_}No.
10020-8{_}2268{_}25 was written at Berwick-on-Tweed; it is an amusing letter, and states how the local agents wanted to put the famous reader into "a little lofty crow's nest," and how "I instantly struck, of course, and said I would either read in a room attached to this house ... or not at all.
10020-8{_}2269{_}Terrified local agents glowered, but fell prostrate."
10020-8{_}2270{_}By the way, notice, in No.
10020-8{_}2271{_}25, the emphasis of gesture on the _me_.
10020-8{_}2272{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2273{_}26.--WRITTEN FEB. 3, 1864.]
10020-8{_}2274{_}[Illustration: DICKENS IN HIS BASKET CARRIAGE.
10020-8{_}2275{_}_From a Photo.
10020-8{_}2276{_}by Mason._]  No.
10020-8{_}2277{_}26 is written in one continuous stroke with a noticeably good management of the curves.
10020-8{_}2278{_}The graceful imagination of this is very striking.
10020-8{_}2279{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2280{_}27.--WRITTEN JUNE 7, 1866.]
10020-8{_}2281{_}[Illustration: CHARLES DICKENS READING TO HIS DAUGHTERS, 1863.
10020-8{_}2282{_}_From a Photograph by R. H. Mason._]  No.
10020-8{_}2283{_}27 shows the endorsement on a cheque.
10020-8{_}2284{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2285{_}28.--WRITTEN JUNE 6, 1870 (THREE DAYS BEFORE DEATH).]
10020-8{_}2286{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2287{_}29.--WRITTEN JUNE 8, 1870 (ONE DAY BEFORE DEATH).]
10020-8{_}2288{_}[Illustration: AGE 56.
10020-8{_}2289{_}_From a Photograph by Garney, New York._]  But we near the end.
10020-8{_}2290{_}Doctors had detected the signs of breaking up, which are not less plain in the written gesture, and had strenuously urged Dickens to stop the incessant strain caused by his public readings.
10020-8{_}2291{_}The stimulus of facing an appreciative audience would spur him on time after time, and then, late at night, he would write affectionate letters giving details of "the house," etc., but which are painful to see if one notices the constant droop of the words and of the lines across the page.
10020-8{_}2292{_}Contrast the writing in No.
10020-8{_}2293{_}28, broken and agitated, with some of the earlier specimens I have shown you.
10020-8{_}2294{_}This was written three days before death.
10020-8{_}2295{_}The wording of the letter from which No.
10020-8{_}2296{_}29 has been copied tells no tale of weakness, but the gesture which clothes the words is tell-tale.
10020-8{_}2297{_}The words, and the lines of words, run downward across the paper, and No.
10020-8{_}2298{_}29 is very suggestive of serious trouble--and it is specially suggestive to those who have studied this form of gesture: look, for example, at the ill-managed flourish.
10020-8{_}2299{_}[Illustration: NO.
10020-8{_}2300{_}30.--WRITTEN JUNE 8, 1870 (ONE DAY BEFORE DEATH.)
10020-8{_}2301{_}_From the last letter written by Charles Dickens._]  Now comes a facsimile taken from the last letter written by Charles Dickens.
10020-8{_}2302{_}It has been given elsewhere, but, not satisfied with the facsimile I saw, I obtained permission to take this direct from the letter in the British Museum.
10020-8{_}2303{_}This was written an hour or so before the fatal seizure.
10020-8{_}2304{_}Every word droops below the level from which each starts, each line of writing descends across the page, the simple _C.
10020-8{_}2305{_}D._ is very shaky, and the whole letter is broken and weak.
10020-8{_}2306{_}Charles Dickens was not "ready" at "3 o'clock"--he died at ten minutes past six p.m. And so ends this too scanty notice of a great man's written-gesture.
10020-8{_}2307{_}*       *       *       *       *  NOTE:--Considerations of space and of the avoidance of technicalities have prevented a really full account of the written gesture of Charles Dickens; scanty as the foregoing account is, the illustrations it contains could not have been supplied by any one collector of Charles Dickens's letters.
10020-8{_}2308{_}I express my sincere gratitude to the many persons who have enabled me to give these illustrations, and only regret that one collector refused my request for the loan of some very early and interesting letters.
10020-8{_}2309{_}J.H.S.
10020-8{_}2310{_}*       *       *       *       *    _The Mirror._  By George Japy.
10020-8{_}2311{_}[Illustration]   It has always been said that the Japanese are the French of the Orient.
10020-8{_}2312{_}Be that as it may, it is very clear that in certain traits which characterize the French, there is no resemblance whatever between the people of those two nations.
10020-8{_}2313{_}Almost as soon as a French baby (a girl, be it understood) is born, its first instinct is to stretch out its tiny hands for a mirror, in which to admire its beautiful little face and its graceful movements.
10020-8{_}2314{_}This natural, and we may say inborn, taste grows with the child's growth, and ere the fair girl has reached her seventeenth year, her ideal of perfect bliss is to find herself in a room with mirrors on every side.
10020-8{_}2315{_}There is indeed a room in the Palace of Versailles which is the elysium of the Frenchwoman.
10020-8{_}2316{_}It is a long room with looking-glasses from ceiling to floor, and the said floor is polished so that it reflects, at any rate, the shadow of the feet.
10020-8{_}2317{_}Now, in the little Japanese village of Yowcuski a looking-glass was an unheard-of thing, and girls did not even know what they looked like, except on hearing the description which their lovers gave them of their personal beauty (which description, by-the-bye, was sometimes slightly biased, according as the lover was more or less devoted).
10020-8{_}2318{_}[Illustration: "HE PICKED UP ONE DAY IN THE STREET A SMALL POCKET HAND-MIRROR."]
10020-8{_}2319{_}Now it happened that a young Japanese, whose daily work was to pull along those light carriages such as were seen at the last Paris Exhibition, picked up one day in the street a small pocket hand-mirror, probably dropped by some English lady-tourist on her travels in that part of the world.
10020-8{_}2320{_}It was, of course, the first time in his life that Kiki-Tsum had ever gazed on such a thing.
10020-8{_}2321{_}He looked carefully at it, and to his intense astonishment saw the image of a brown face, with dark, intelligent eyes, and a look of awestruck wonderment expressed on its features.
10020-8{_}2322{_}Kiki-Tsum dropped on his knees, and gazing earnestly at the object he held in his hand, he whispered, "It is my sainted father.
10020-8{_}2323{_}How could his portrait have come here?
10020-8{_}2324{_}Is it, perhaps, a warning of some kind for me?"
10020-8{_}2325{_}He carefully folded the precious treasure up in his handkerchief, and put it in the large pocket of his loose blouse.
10020-8{_}2326{_}When he went home that night he hid it away carefully in a vase which was scarcely ever touched, as he did not know of any safer place in which to deposit it.
10020-8{_}2327{_}He said nothing of the adventure to his young wife, for, as he said to himself "Women are curious, and then, too, _sometimes_ they are given to talking," and Kiki-Tsum felt that it was too reverent a matter to be discussed by neighbours, this finding of his dead father's portrait in the street.
10020-8{_}2328{_}For some days Kiki-Tsum was in a great state of excitement.
10020-8{_}2329{_}He was thinking of the portrait all the time, and at intervals he would leave his work and suddenly appear at home to take a furtive look at his treasure.
10020-8{_}2330{_}[Illustration: "ALWAYS WITH THE SAME SOLEMN EXPRESSION."]
10020-8{_}2331{_}Now, in Japan, as in other countries, mysterious actions and irregular proceedings of all kinds have to be explained to a wife.
10020-8{_}2332{_}Lili-Tsee did not understand why her husband kept appearing at all hours of the day.
10020-8{_}2333{_}Certainly he kissed her every time he came in like this.
10020-8{_}2334{_}At first she was satisfied with his explanation when he told her that he only ran in for a minute to see her pretty face.
10020-8{_}2335{_}She thought it was really quite natural on his part, but when day after day he appeared, and always with the same solemn expression on his face, she began to wonder in her heart of hearts whether he was telling her the whole truth.
10020-8{_}2336{_}And so Lili-Tsee fell to watching her husband's movements, and she noticed that he never went away until he had been alone in the little room at the back of the house.
10020-8{_}2337{_}[Illustration: "WHAT WAS IT SHE SAW?"]
10020-8{_}2338{_}Now the Japanese women are as persevering as any others when there is a mystery to be discovered, and so Lili-Tsee set herself to discover this mystery.
10020-8{_}2339{_}She hunted day after day to see if she could find some trace of anything in that little room which was at all unusual, but she found nothing.
10020-8{_}2340{_}One day, however, she happened to come in suddenly and saw her husband replacing the long blue vase in which she kept her rose leaves in order to dry them.
10020-8{_}2341{_}He made some excuse about its not looking very steady, and appeared to be just setting it right, and Lili-Tsee pretended there was nothing out of the common in his putting the vase straight.
10020-8{_}2342{_}The moment he had gone out of the house, though, she was up on a stool like lightning, and in a moment she had fished the looking-glass out of the vase.
10020-8{_}2343{_}She took it carefully in her hand, wondering whatever it could be, but when she looked in it the terrible truth was clear.
10020-8{_}2344{_}What was it she saw?
10020-8{_}2345{_}Why, the portrait of a woman, and she had believed that Kiki-Tsum was so good, and so fond, and so true.
10020-8{_}2346{_}Her grief was at first too deep for any words.
10020-8{_}2347{_}She just sat down on the floor with the terrible portrait in her lap, and rocked herself backwards and forwards.
10020-8{_}2348{_}This, then, was why her husband came home so many times in the day.
10020-8{_}2349{_}It was to look at the portrait of the woman she had just seen.
10020-8{_}2350{_}Suddenly a fit of anger seized her, and she gazed at the glass again.
10020-8{_}2351{_}The same face looked at her, but she wondered how her husband could admire such a face, so wicked did the dark eyes look: there was an expression in them that she certainly had not seen the first time she had looked at it, and it terrified her so much that she made up her mind not to look at it again.
10020-8{_}2352{_}She had no heart, however, for anything, and did not even make any attempt to prepare a meal for her husband.
10020-8{_}2353{_}She just went on sitting there on the floor, nursing the portrait, and at the same time her wrath.
10020-8{_}2354{_}When later on Kiki-Tsum arrived, he was surprised to find nothing ready for their evening meal, and no wife.
10020-8{_}2355{_}He walked through to the other rooms, and was not long left in ignorance of the cause of the unusual state of things.
10020-8{_}2356{_}"So this is the love you professed for me!
10020-8{_}2357{_}This is the way in which you treat me, before we have even been married a year!"
10020-8{_}2358{_}"What do you mean, Lili-Tsee?"
10020-8{_}2359{_}asked her husband, in consternation, thinking that his poor wife had taken leave of her senses.
10020-8{_}2360{_}"What do I mean?
10020-8{_}2361{_}What do you mean?
10020-8{_}2362{_}I should think.
10020-8{_}2363{_}The idea of your keeping portraits in my rose-leaf vase.
10020-8{_}2364{_}Here, take it and treasure it, for I do not want it, the wicked, wicked woman!"
10020-8{_}2365{_}and here poor Lili-Tsee burst out crying.
10020-8{_}2366{_}"I cannot understand," said her bewildered husband.
10020-8{_}2367{_}"Oh, you can't?"
10020-8{_}2368{_}she said, laughing hysterically.
10020-8{_}2369{_}"I can, though, well enough.
10020-8{_}2370{_}You like that hideous, villainous-looking woman better than your own true wife.
10020-8{_}2371{_}I would say nothing if she were at any rate beautiful; but she has a vile face, a hideous face, and looks wicked and murderous, and everything that is bad!"
10020-8{_}2372{_}"Lili-Tsee, what do you mean?"
10020-8{_}2373{_}asked her husband, getting exasperated in his turn.
10020-8{_}2374{_}"That portrait is the living image of my poor dead father.
10020-8{_}2375{_}I found it in the street the other day, and put it in your vase for safety."
10020-8{_}2376{_}Lili-Tsee's eyes flashed with indignation at this apparently barefaced lie.
10020-8{_}2377{_}"Hear him!"
10020-8{_}2378{_}she almost screamed.
10020-8{_}2379{_}"He wants to tell me now that I do not know a woman's face from a man's."
10020-8{_}2380{_}Kiki-Tsum was wild with indignation, and a quarrel began in good earnest.
10020-8{_}2381{_}The street-door was a little way open, and the loud, angry words attracted the notice of a _bonze_ (one of the Japanese priests) who happened to be passing.
10020-8{_}2382{_}"My children," he said, putting his head in at the door, "why this unseemly anger, why this dispute?"
10020-8{_}2383{_}"Father," said Kiki-Tsum, "my wife is mad."
10020-8{_}2384{_}"All women are so, my son, more or less," interrupted the holy _bonze_.
10020-8{_}2385{_}"You were wrong to expect perfection, and must abide by your bargain now.
10020-8{_}2386{_}It is no use getting angry, all wives are trials."
10020-8{_}2387{_}"But what she says is a lie."
10020-8{_}2388{_}"It is not, father," exclaimed Lili-Tsee.
10020-8{_}2389{_}"My husband has the portrait of a woman, and I found it hidden in my rose-leaf vase."
10020-8{_}2390{_}"I swear that I have no portrait but that of my poor dead father," explained the aggrieved husband.
10020-8{_}2391{_}"My children, my children," said the holy _bonze_, majestically, "show me the portraits."
10020-8{_}2392{_}"Here it is; there is only one, but it is one too many," said Lili-Tsee, sarcastically.
10020-8{_}2393{_}The _bonze_ took the glass and looked at it earnestly.
10020-8{_}2394{_}He then bowed low before it, and in an altered tone said: "My children, settle your quarrel and live peaceably together.
10020-8{_}2395{_}You are both in the wrong.
10020-8{_}2396{_}This portrait is that of a saintly and venerable _bonze_.
10020-8{_}2397{_}I know not how you could mistake so holy a face.
10020-8{_}2398{_}I must take it from you and place it amongst the precious relics of our church."
10020-8{_}2399{_}So saying, the _bonze_ lifted his hands to bless the husband and wife, and then went slowly away, carrying with him the glass which had wrought such mischief.
10020-8{_}2400{_}END.
10020-8{_}2401{_}[Illustration]         *       *       *       *       *    _Handcuffs._  WRITTEN AND ILLUSTRATED BY INSPECTOR MAURICE MOSER,  _Late of the Criminal Investigation Department, Great Scotland Yard._   The ordinary connection of ideas between handcuffs and policemen does not need very acute mental powers to grasp, but there is a further connection, a philological one, which is only evident at first sight to those who have made a small acquaintance with the science of words.
10020-8{_}2402{_}The word "handcuff" is a popular corruption of the Anglo-Saxon "handcop," _i.e._, that which "cops" or "catches" the hands.
10020-8{_}2403{_}Now, one of the most common of the many slang expressions used by their special enemies towards the police is "Copper"--_i.e._, he who cops the offending member.
10020-8{_}2404{_}Strange as it may seem, handcuffs are by no means the invention of these times, which insist on making the life of a prisoner so devoid of the picturesque and romantic.
10020-8{_}2405{_}We must go back, past the dark ages, past the stirring times of Greek and Roman antiquity, till we come to those blissful mythological ages when every tree and every stream was the home of some kindly god.
10020-8{_}2406{_}In those olden days there dwelt in the Carpathian Sea a wily old deity, known by the name of Proteus, possessing the gift of prophecy, the fruits of which he selfishly denied to mankind.
